Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
The Maximum and Minimum Duration of Pregnancy: Proofs from Quran and Hadith
Ruling with proof on pregnancy duration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the maximum (two years) and minimum (six months) duration of pregnancy and identify the primary proofs from the hadith of Aisha and the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
A child remains in the womb at most two years and at least six months, established by a hadith and the thirty-month verse.

In this passage, the author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the foundational Hanafi ruling on the duration of pregnancy. He cites both a hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) and a Quranic verse to establish the maximum and minimum limits. Understanding these proofs is essential for applying the rules of lineage in various marital and ownership situations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The author states: 'The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years, its minimum is six months.' This is the Hanafi position.
2
Locate the hadith proof
The hadith from Aisha: 'The child does not remain in the womb for more than two years, even if for the length of a spindle's shadow.' Narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi.
The spindle shadow metaphor emphasizes extreme brevity.
3
Understand the minimum proof
The Quranic verse (46:15) says gestation and weaning are thirty months; another verse (31:14) says weaning is two years. Subtracting 24 months from 30 leaves six months for gestation.
This deduction is attributed to the scholars.
4
Address the apparent contradiction
There is a report about Abu Hanifa that the thirty-month period is entire for both, but the hadith of Aisha reduces gestation to six months. The author defends the hadith as stronger.
Shows Hanafi method of reconciling proofs.

بظل مغزل. رواه الدارقطني والبيهقي. وهو لا يعرف إلا سماعاً. وظل المغزل مثل لقلته لأن ظله حالة الدوران أسرع زوالاً من سائر الظلال وهو على حذف المضاف تقديره ولو بقدر ظل مغزل. ويروى ولو بفلكة مغزل أي ولو بقدر دوران فلكة مغزل. قوله: (وأقلها ستة أشهر) لقوله تعالى: (وحمله وفصاله ثلاثون شهراً) [الأحقاف:15] ثم قال: (وفصاله في عامين) [لقمان:14] فيبقى للحمل ستة أشهر؛ كذا في الهداية. وقد نقل في فتح القدير أنه لا خلاف للعلماء فيه وأورد على ما في الهداية أنه مخالف لما قرره لأبي حنيفة في الرضاع من أن هذه المدة مضروبة بتمامها لكل من الحمل والفصال غير أن المنقص قام في أحدهما الحمل وهو حديث عائشة عنها. قلنا: قدمنا هناك أنه غير صحيح لما يلزم من أنه يراد بلفظ الثلاثين في إطلاق واحد حقيقة ثلاثين وأربعة وعشرين باعتبار إضافتين فلعله رجع إلى الصحيح. قوله: (فلو نكح أمة فطلقها فاشتراها فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منه أي من وقت الشراء) قوله: (لزمه وإلا لا) أي وإن ولدت لتمام ستة أشهر أو لأكثر منها لا يلزمه لأن في الوجه الأول ولد المعتدة فإن العلوق سابق على الشراء، وفي الوجه الثاني ولد المملوكة لأنه يضاف الحادث إلى أقرب وقته حيث لم يتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى. وبه اندفع ما أورد عليه كما علم في فتح القدير فلا بد من دعوته واقتصار الشارح على الأكثر في قوله «وإلا لا» ينبغي وقد صرح في فتح القدير بما ذكرناه وأطلق في الأمة فشمل المدخول بها وغيرها كما أطلق في الطلاق فشمل الرجعي والبائن الواحدة والثنتين وكل من الإطلاقين غير صحيح فإن كان بعد الدخول فلا فرق بين الرجعي والبائن إذا كان واحدة، وإن كان قبل الدخول ولأقل من سنتين منه، فالإيجاب على إبهامه ولا تتعين ضرتها للطلاق؛ ذكره في الزيادات. وثانيها ما لو قال لها إذا حبلت فأنت طالق فولدت لأقل من سنتين من وقت التعليق لا يقع الطلاق، وكذا لو كان هذا في تعليق العتاق بالحبل وثالثها المطلقة الرجعية إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لا يصير مراجعاً، ولو كانت الحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات لثبتت هذه الأحكام أعني البيان والطلاق والرجعة قلنا الحوادث إنما تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات إذا لم تتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى أما إذا تضمن فلا فمتى عولت على ما قلنا ثم استقريت المسائل فإنه لا يلزمه الولد إلا أن تجيء بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق إذا ولدت لتمام ستة أشهر أو أكثر من وقت التزوج. وفي غاية البيان : ولنا فيه نظر لأن الطلاق قبل الدخول بائن والحكم في المبانة أن نسب ولدها يثبت إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق، نعم إن محمداً وضع في الجامع الصغير في المدخول بها اهـ وجوابه أن هذا حكم المبانة إذا كانت معتدة وغير المدخول بها لا عدة عليها، وأما إذا كان الطلاق ثنتين فإنه يمتد نسب الولد إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق، وإن لم يدع فإن ولدت لأكثر من ذلك لا يثبت إلا إذا ادعاه لحرمتها حرمة غليظة فيضاف العلوق إلى أبعد الأوقات وهو ما قبل الطلاق حملاً لأمرهما على الصلاح. وذكر في غاية البيان أن في التقييد بالثنتين لهذا الحكم إيهاماً لأنه ربما يظن ظان أن الطلاق إذا كان واحداً بائناً لا يثبت النسب فيه إلى سنتين وليس كذلك لأن النسب في البائن يثبت إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق وإن لم يدع .اهـ وجوابه بالفرق بين البينونة الخفيفة وبين الغليظة فإن في الخفيفة يعتبر وقت الشراء أيضاً وهو أن تلده لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء وإذا كان من سنتين من وقت الطلاق وفي الغليظة لا يعتبر ذلك حتى لو ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء ولسنتين من وقت الطلاق ثبت نسبه بلا دعوة فظهر الفرق والإيهام في فهمه في كلام المشايخ فالحاصل أنه يستثنى من حكم المسألة المذكورة في المختصر المطلقة قبل الدخول والمبانة بالثنتين فإن فيهما لا اعتبار لوقت الشراء وإنما يعتبر وقت الطلاق، ففي الأولى يشترط لثبوت نسبه ولادته لأقل من ستة أشهر، وفي الثانية لسنتين فأقل، وقد علم مما قدمه المصنف أن هذه الأمة لو كان طلاقها رجعياً فإنه يثبت نسب ولدها وإن جاءت به لعشر سنين بعد الطلاق أو أكثر، وإن كان بائناً فلا بد أن تأتي به لتمام سنتين أو أقل بعد أن يكون ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء في المسألتين فلا يرد عليه ما إذا أتت به المبتوتة لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الطلاق ولأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء وإن كان داخلاً في عبارته هنا لما قدمه سابقاً، والتقييد بالطلاق اتفاقي لأن الحكم فيما إذا لم يطلقها واشتراها كذلك أي لا لأقل من ستة أشهر منه ففي ثبوت الطلاق في الصورتين الأوليين إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بيقين فلا يعين وفي الرجعة كذلك العمل بخلاف الدليل الدال على استكراه الرجعة بغير القول قوله : (ثبت نسبه بلا دعوة) لأنه ولد معتدته لا مملوكته لأنه لا يمكن حمله على أنها علقت به بعد الشراء لأن ملكه لها لا يحلها له بعد الحرمة الغليظة حتى تنكح غيره بخلاف ما لو كانت حرمة خفيفة بأن طلقها بعد الدخول واحدة بائنة فإذا شراها يحل له وطؤها لأنها معتدة منه وعدتها منه لا تحرمها عليه، فإذا ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر احتمل كونه بعد الشراء فيضاف إليه لأنه أقرب والحادث يضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته فيكون ولد مملوكته فلا يثبت بلا دعوة قوله : ( وفي الثانية لسنتين فأقل مخالف لما مشى عليه فيما مر ولد معتدة البت لا يثبت إلا إذا أتت به لأقل من سنتين فينبغي أن يكون هنا كذلك كما قاله بعض الفضلاء، وقد قدمنا عن النهر الخلاف في ذلك وأنه محمول على اختلاف الرواية فيمكن أن
The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years, its minimum is six months. So if a man married a slave woman, then divorced her, then bought her, and she gave birth at less than six months from him (i.e., from the time of purchase), it is binding upon him; otherwise not. He said: (The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years) on the authority of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: The child does not remain in the womb for more than two years, even if for the length of a spindle's shadow. Narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. It is not known except by hearsay. The shadow of a spindle is like spinning because its shadow during rotation is faster to vanish than other shadows. He said: (Its minimum is six months) due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And his gestation and weaning are thirty months' [Quran 46:15], then He said: 'And his weaning in two years' [Quran 31:14], so six months remain for pregnancy. Thus in al-Hidayah. In Fath al-Qadir it is transmitted that there is no disagreement among scholars on this, and an objection was raised against what is in al-Hidayah that it contradicts what he established for Abu Hanifa regarding breastfeeding, that this period is applied in its entirety for both pregnancy and weaning, but the reduction occurred in one of them, pregnancy, and that is the hadith of Aisha. We said: We have presented there that it is not valid because it would entail that the word thirty is used in one utterance to mean both thirty and twenty-four depending on two attributions, so perhaps he retracted to the correct view. He said: (So if he married a slave woman, then divorced her, then bought her, and she gave birth at less than six months from him, i.e., from the time of purchase) He said: (It is binding upon him; otherwise not) meaning if she gives birth at a full six months or more, it is not binding upon him because in the first case it is the child of the waiting woman ('iddah), since conception preceded the purchase; in the second case it is the child of the slave woman (owned by him), because the event is attributed to the nearest possible time since it does not entail invalidating what was established by proof or abandoning the implied meaning. And thus the objection is repelled. So دعوته (his claim) is necessary. The commentator's restriction to 'the majority' in his statement 'otherwise not' is appropriate. And it is stated in Fath al-Qadir as we mentioned. He made absolute in 'slave woman' to include the one entered upon and the one not entered upon, and he made absolute in 'divorce' to include revocable and irrevocable, one and two, but both absolutes are not correct. If after consummation, there is no difference between revocable and irrevocable if it is one. If before consummation, and for less than two years from it, then the obligation is according to its ambiguity and his co-wife is not specified for divorce; mentioned in al-Ziyadat. The second scenario: if he said to her, 'If you become pregnant, you are divorced,' and she gave birth within less than two years from the time of the conditional statement, the divorce does not occur. Similarly if this were in the conditional suspension of manumission upon pregnancy. The third scenario: the revocably divorced woman if she comes with a child within less than two years from the time of divorce, he does not become a revocator. If events were attributed to the nearest times, these rulings would be established, namely expression, divorce, and return. We say: Events are only attributed to the nearest times if they do not entail invalidating what was established by proof or abandoning the implied meaning; if they entail that, then not. So whenever you rely on what we said and then examine the issues, he is not obliged to accept the child unless she comes with the child at less than six months from the time of divorce if she gives birth at a full six months or more from the time of marriage. In Ghayat al-Bayan: We have a consideration in this, because divorce before consummation is irrevocable, and the ruling for the irrevocably divorced woman is that the lineage of her child is established up to two years from the time of divorce, but Muhammad placed in al-Jami' al-Saghir concerning the one entered upon. The answer is that this is the ruling for the irrevocably divorced woman if she has an 'iddah, but the one not entered upon has no 'iddah. As for when the divorce is two (i.e., three pronouncements in total), the lineage of the child extends up to two years from the time of divorce, even if he does not claim. If she gives birth after that, it is not established unless he claims it, because of the severe prohibition, so conception is attributed to the furthest time, i.e., before divorce, to reconcile their affair. It is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that restricting this ruling to two divorces creates an ambiguity, because one might think that if the divorce is one and irrevocable, lineage is not established up to two years, but that is not the case because lineage in irrevocable divorce is established up to two years from the time of divorce even without a claim. The answer is the distinction between light and severe prohibition: in light prohibition, the time of purchase is also considered, meaning she gives birth at less than six months from the time of purchase and within two years from the time of divorce; in severe prohibition, that consideration is not given, so even if she gives birth at more than six months from the time of purchase and within two years from the time of divorce, his lineage is established without a claim. Thus the difference becomes clear, and the ambiguity is in the understanding of the scholars' statements. The conclusion: exceptions to the ruling mentioned in al-Mukhtasar are: the woman divorced before consummation and the one divorced with three pronouncements; in these two, the time of purchase is not considered, only the time of divorce. So in the first, for his lineage to be established, she must give birth at less than six months; in the second, two years or less. It has been known from what the author presented earlier that if this slave woman's divorce was revocable, his lineage is established even if she comes with the child ten years after the divorce or more. If it was irrevocable, she must come with it within a full two years or less after being six months from the time of purchase in both issues. So what is claimed against him, that if the permanently divorced woman comes with a child more than two years from divorce but less than six months from purchase, even if it is within his expression here, is not a problem because of what he presented earlier. The restriction to divorce is coincidental, because the ruling when he did not divorce her and bought her is the same, i.e., not for less than six months from it. So in establishing divorce in the first two pictures, it invalidates what was established with certainty, so it does not help. In return (رجعة) similarly, working contrary to the evidence indicating disapproval of return without uttering a statement. He said: (His lineage is established without a claim) because he is the child of his woman in 'iddah, not his slave woman, because it cannot be supposed that she conceived after the purchase, since his ownership of her does not make her permissible for him after severe prohibition until she marries another. Unlike if it were a light prohibition, where he divorced her after consummation with one irrevocable divorce, then when he buys her, it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her because she is in 'iddah from him and her 'iddah from him does not prohibit her to him. So if she gives birth more than six months, it is possible that it was after the purchase, so it is attributed to it because it is nearest, and the event is attributed to its nearest time, so it is the child of his slave woman and is not established without a claim. He said: (And in the second, two years or less) contradicts what he proceeded with earlier: the child of the irrevocably divorced woman is not established unless she comes with it within less than two years, so it should be the same here as some scholars said. And we have presented from al-Nahr the disagreement on that and that it is carried on variance of narration, so it is possible that

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the rulings on the duration of pregnancy and its implications for establishing lineage. The author begins by stating the agreed-upon Hanafi position that the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years, based on the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that a child does not remain in the womb beyond two years, even for the length of a spindle's shadow—an expression of extreme brevity. The minimum is six months, derived from the Quranic verse: 'And his gestation and weaning are thirty months' [46:15], coupled with 'And his weaning is in two years' [31:14], leaving six months for gestation. The author then addresses a potential contradiction regarding Imam Abu Hanifa's view on breastfeeding, explaining that the thirty-month period is to be interpreted as the full span for both gestation and weaning, but the reduction occurs in gestation due to the hadith evidence. The central ruling is then presented: if a man marries a slave woman, divorces her, then purchases her, and she gives birth less than six months after the purchase, the child's lineage is established and he must acknowledge it; if she gives birth at six months or more, it is not established unless he makes a claim. This is because in the first case, the child is considered the offspring of the 'iddah period (conception before purchase), whereas in the second case, the child is attributed to the nearest possible conception time (after purchase), making the child the offspring of his owned slave woman, which requires a claim for lineage. The passage then discusses three subsidiary scenarios: (1) when a man tells his slave woman, 'If there is a child in your womb, it is from me,' and she testifies accordingly, requiring further evidence if contested; (2) conditional divorce tied to pregnancy, where if she gives birth within two years of the conditional statement, the divorce does not occur because the nearest conception time is not considered for rulings that would invalidate established principles; (3) a revocably divorced woman who gives birth within two years does not automatically effectuate a return (raj'a) because that would involve applying a ruling based on the nearest time that contradicts the intended legal consequence. The underlying principle is that events are attributed to the nearest possible time only when doing so does not invalidate what is established by definitive evidence or require abandoning the implied legal meaning (muqtada). The passage concludes by distinguishing between forms of divorce (before consummation, after consummation, with one or two pronouncements) and their effect on the time period for establishing lineage, noting the difference between 'light' and 'severe' prohibition (bainuna) in terms of considering the purchase time for the slave woman's child.

Key Terms
مدة الحمل (muddat al-haml): The period of time from conception to birth; in this text, the maximum is two years and the minimum six months.
ظل مغزل (zill mighzal): The shadow of a spindle; used metaphorically to denote an extremely brief period of time, emphasizing that no child stays in the womb beyond two years even for the briefest moment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum duration of pregnancy according to the Hanafi school?
A) 6 months
B) 9 months
C) 12 months
D) 2 years

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school sets the maximum pregnancy at two years and the minimum at six months, based on the hadith of Aisha and the Quranic verse on gestation and weaning.

Talaq — Divorce

Applying the Pregnancy Duration: Lineage in Cases of Divorce and Purchase of a Slave Woman
Application of pregnancy duration to lineage disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the pregnancy duration rules to determine the lineage of a child born after the divorce and purchase of a slave woman, distinguishing between revocable and irrevocable divorce, and the effect of consummation.

Core Idea
When a man buys his divorced slave woman, the child's lineage is automatically established if born within six months of purchase; otherwise, it requires his claim.

This lesson applies the pregnancy duration rulings to a specific scenario: a man marries a slave woman, divorces her, then purchases her, and she gives birth. The author explains how to determine whether the child is attributed to the marriage (as an 'iddah child) or to the ownership (as a mawlud slave child) based on the time of birth relative to the purchase. The lesson also covers important exceptions for different types of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The scenario: a man marries a slave woman, divorces her (any type), then buys her, and she gives birth. The ruling depends on the time between purchase and birth.
2
Apply the six-month rule
If she gives birth less than six months after purchase, the child is considered conceived during the 'iddah (from the marriage), so lineage is automatically established. If at six months or more, the child is attributed to the purchase (ownership), so lineage requires a claim from the man.
3
Consider exceptions for divorce types
The rule is general, but exceptions exist: if the divorce was before consummation (no 'iddah), the time of purchase is not considered; only the time from divorce (less than six months) matters. For a woman divorced with three pronouncements (severe prohibition), the time from divorce is two years, ignoring the purchase time.
4
Understand the principle of attributing events to nearest time
The general principle is that events are attributed to the nearest possible time. However, this principle is not applied when it would invalidate established proofs or contradict the legal intent.

Source Text

بظل مغزل. رواه الدارقطني والبيهقي. وهو لا يعرف إلا سماعاً. وظل المغزل مثل لقلته لأن ظله حالة الدوران أسرع زوالاً من سائر الظلال وهو على حذف المضاف تقديره ولو بقدر ظل مغزل. ويروى ولو بفلكة مغزل أي ولو بقدر دوران فلكة مغزل. قوله: (وأقلها ستة أشهر) لقوله تعالى: (وحمله وفصاله ثلاثون شهراً) [الأحقاف:15] ثم قال: (وفصاله في عامين) [لقمان:14] فيبقى للحمل ستة أشهر؛ كذا في الهداية. وقد نقل في فتح القدير أنه لا خلاف للعلماء فيه وأورد على ما في الهداية أنه مخالف لما قرره لأبي حنيفة في الرضاع من أن هذه المدة مضروبة بتمامها لكل من الحمل والفصال غير أن المنقص قام في أحدهما الحمل وهو حديث عائشة عنها. قلنا: قدمنا هناك أنه غير صحيح لما يلزم من أنه يراد بلفظ الثلاثين في إطلاق واحد حقيقة ثلاثين وأربعة وعشرين باعتبار إضافتين فلعله رجع إلى الصحيح. قوله: (فلو نكح أمة فطلقها فاشتراها فولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر منه أي من وقت الشراء) قوله: (لزمه وإلا لا) أي وإن ولدت لتمام ستة أشهر أو لأكثر منها لا يلزمه لأن في الوجه الأول ولد المعتدة فإن العلوق سابق على الشراء، وفي الوجه الثاني ولد المملوكة لأنه يضاف الحادث إلى أقرب وقته حيث لم يتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى. وبه اندفع ما أورد عليه كما علم في فتح القدير فلا بد من دعوته واقتصار الشارح على الأكثر في قوله «وإلا لا» ينبغي وقد صرح في فتح القدير بما ذكرناه وأطلق في الأمة فشمل المدخول بها وغيرها كما أطلق في الطلاق فشمل الرجعي والبائن الواحدة والثنتين وكل من الإطلاقين غير صحيح فإن كان بعد الدخول فلا فرق بين الرجعي والبائن إذا كان واحدة، وإن كان قبل الدخول ولأقل من سنتين منه، فالإيجاب على إبهامه ولا تتعين ضرتها للطلاق؛ ذكره في الزيادات. وثانيها ما لو قال لها إذا حبلت فأنت طالق فولدت لأقل من سنتين من وقت التعليق لا يقع الطلاق، وكذا لو كان هذا في تعليق العتاق بالحبل وثالثها المطلقة الرجعية إذا جاءت به لأقل من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لا يصير مراجعاً، ولو كانت الحوادث تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات لثبتت هذه الأحكام أعني البيان والطلاق والرجعة قلنا الحوادث إنما تضاف إلى أقرب الأوقات إذا لم تتضمن إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بالدليل أو ترك العمل بالمقتضى أما إذا تضمن فلا فمتى عولت على ما قلنا ثم استقريت المسائل فإنه لا يلزمه الولد إلا أن تجيء بالولد لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق إذا ولدت لتمام ستة أشهر أو أكثر من وقت التزوج. وفي غاية البيان : ولنا فيه نظر لأن الطلاق قبل الدخول بائن والحكم في المبانة أن نسب ولدها يثبت إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق، نعم إن محمداً وضع في الجامع الصغير في المدخول بها اهـ وجوابه أن هذا حكم المبانة إذا كانت معتدة وغير المدخول بها لا عدة عليها، وأما إذا كان الطلاق ثنتين فإنه يمتد نسب الولد إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق، وإن لم يدع فإن ولدت لأكثر من ذلك لا يثبت إلا إذا ادعاه لحرمتها حرمة غليظة فيضاف العلوق إلى أبعد الأوقات وهو ما قبل الطلاق حملاً لأمرهما على الصلاح. وذكر في غاية البيان أن في التقييد بالثنتين لهذا الحكم إيهاماً لأنه ربما يظن ظان أن الطلاق إذا كان واحداً بائناً لا يثبت النسب فيه إلى سنتين وليس كذلك لأن النسب في البائن يثبت إلى سنتين من وقت الطلاق وإن لم يدع .اهـ وجوابه بالفرق بين البينونة الخفيفة وبين الغليظة فإن في الخفيفة يعتبر وقت الشراء أيضاً وهو أن تلده لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء وإذا كان من سنتين من وقت الطلاق وفي الغليظة لا يعتبر ذلك حتى لو ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء ولسنتين من وقت الطلاق ثبت نسبه بلا دعوة فظهر الفرق والإيهام في فهمه في كلام المشايخ فالحاصل أنه يستثنى من حكم المسألة المذكورة في المختصر المطلقة قبل الدخول والمبانة بالثنتين فإن فيهما لا اعتبار لوقت الشراء وإنما يعتبر وقت الطلاق، ففي الأولى يشترط لثبوت نسبه ولادته لأقل من ستة أشهر، وفي الثانية لسنتين فأقل، وقد علم مما قدمه المصنف أن هذه الأمة لو كان طلاقها رجعياً فإنه يثبت نسب ولدها وإن جاءت به لعشر سنين بعد الطلاق أو أكثر، وإن كان بائناً فلا بد أن تأتي به لتمام سنتين أو أقل بعد أن يكون ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء في المسألتين فلا يرد عليه ما إذا أتت به المبتوتة لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الطلاق ولأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الشراء وإن كان داخلاً في عبارته هنا لما قدمه سابقاً، والتقييد بالطلاق اتفاقي لأن الحكم فيما إذا لم يطلقها واشتراها كذلك أي لا لأقل من ستة أشهر منه ففي ثبوت الطلاق في الصورتين الأوليين إبطال ما كان ثابتاً بيقين فلا يعين وفي الرجعة كذلك العمل بخلاف الدليل الدال على استكراه الرجعة بغير القول قوله : (ثبت نسبه بلا دعوة) لأنه ولد معتدته لا مملوكته لأنه لا يمكن حمله على أنها علقت به بعد الشراء لأن ملكه لها لا يحلها له بعد الحرمة الغليظة حتى تنكح غيره بخلاف ما لو كانت حرمة خفيفة بأن طلقها بعد الدخول واحدة بائنة فإذا شراها يحل له وطؤها لأنها معتدة منه وعدتها منه لا تحرمها عليه، فإذا ولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر احتمل كونه بعد الشراء فيضاف إليه لأنه أقرب والحادث يضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته فيكون ولد مملوكته فلا يثبت بلا دعوة قوله : ( وفي الثانية لسنتين فأقل مخالف لما مشى عليه فيما مر ولد معتدة البت لا يثبت إلا إذا أتت به لأقل من سنتين فينبغي أن يكون هنا كذلك كما قاله بعض الفضلاء، وقد قدمنا عن النهر الخلاف في ذلك وأنه محمول على اختلاف الرواية فيمكن أن
The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years, its minimum is six months. So if a man married a slave woman, then divorced her, then bought her, and she gave birth at less than six months from him (i.e., from the time of purchase), it is binding upon him; otherwise not. He said: (The maximum duration of pregnancy is two years) on the authority of Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: The child does not remain in the womb for more than two years, even if for the length of a spindle's shadow. Narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi. It is not known except by hearsay. The shadow of a spindle is like spinning because its shadow during rotation is faster to vanish than other shadows. He said: (Its minimum is six months) due to His statement, the Exalted: 'And his gestation and weaning are thirty months' [Quran 46:15], then He said: 'And his weaning in two years' [Quran 31:14], so six months remain for pregnancy. Thus in al-Hidayah. In Fath al-Qadir it is transmitted that there is no disagreement among scholars on this, and an objection was raised against what is in al-Hidayah that it contradicts what he established for Abu Hanifa regarding breastfeeding, that this period is applied in its entirety for both pregnancy and weaning, but the reduction occurred in one of them, pregnancy, and that is the hadith of Aisha. We said: We have presented there that it is not valid because it would entail that the word thirty is used in one utterance to mean both thirty and twenty-four depending on two attributions, so perhaps he retracted to the correct view. He said: (So if he married a slave woman, then divorced her, then bought her, and she gave birth at less than six months from him, i.e., from the time of purchase) He said: (It is binding upon him; otherwise not) meaning if she gives birth at a full six months or more, it is not binding upon him because in the first case it is the child of the waiting woman ('iddah), since conception preceded the purchase; in the second case it is the child of the slave woman (owned by him), because the event is attributed to the nearest possible time since it does not entail invalidating what was established by proof or abandoning the implied meaning. And thus the objection is repelled. So دعوته (his claim) is necessary. The commentator's restriction to 'the majority' in his statement 'otherwise not' is appropriate. And it is stated in Fath al-Qadir as we mentioned. He made absolute in 'slave woman' to include the one entered upon and the one not entered upon, and he made absolute in 'divorce' to include revocable and irrevocable, one and two, but both absolutes are not correct. If after consummation, there is no difference between revocable and irrevocable if it is one. If before consummation, and for less than two years from it, then the obligation is according to its ambiguity and his co-wife is not specified for divorce; mentioned in al-Ziyadat. The second scenario: if he said to her, 'If you become pregnant, you are divorced,' and she gave birth within less than two years from the time of the conditional statement, the divorce does not occur. Similarly if this were in the conditional suspension of manumission upon pregnancy. The third scenario: the revocably divorced woman if she comes with a child within less than two years from the time of divorce, he does not become a revocator. If events were attributed to the nearest times, these rulings would be established, namely expression, divorce, and return. We say: Events are only attributed to the nearest times if they do not entail invalidating what was established by proof or abandoning the implied meaning; if they entail that, then not. So whenever you rely on what we said and then examine the issues, he is not obliged to accept the child unless she comes with the child at less than six months from the time of divorce if she gives birth at a full six months or more from the time of marriage. In Ghayat al-Bayan: We have a consideration in this, because divorce before consummation is irrevocable, and the ruling for the irrevocably divorced woman is that the lineage of her child is established up to two years from the time of divorce, but Muhammad placed in al-Jami' al-Saghir concerning the one entered upon. The answer is that this is the ruling for the irrevocably divorced woman if she has an 'iddah, but the one not entered upon has no 'iddah. As for when the divorce is two (i.e., three pronouncements in total), the lineage of the child extends up to two years from the time of divorce, even if he does not claim. If she gives birth after that, it is not established unless he claims it, because of the severe prohibition, so conception is attributed to the furthest time, i.e., before divorce, to reconcile their affair. It is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that restricting this ruling to two divorces creates an ambiguity, because one might think that if the divorce is one and irrevocable, lineage is not established up to two years, but that is not the case because lineage in irrevocable divorce is established up to two years from the time of divorce even without a claim. The answer is the distinction between light and severe prohibition: in light prohibition, the time of purchase is also considered, meaning she gives birth at less than six months from the time of purchase and within two years from the time of divorce; in severe prohibition, that consideration is not given, so even if she gives birth at more than six months from the time of purchase and within two years from the time of divorce, his lineage is established without a claim. Thus the difference becomes clear, and the ambiguity is in the understanding of the scholars' statements. The conclusion: exceptions to the ruling mentioned in al-Mukhtasar are: the woman divorced before consummation and the one divorced with three pronouncements; in these two, the time of purchase is not considered, only the time of divorce. So in the first, for his lineage to be established, she must give birth at less than six months; in the second, two years or less. It has been known from what the author presented earlier that if this slave woman's divorce was revocable, his lineage is established even if she comes with the child ten years after the divorce or more. If it was irrevocable, she must come with it within a full two years or less after being six months from the time of purchase in both issues. So what is claimed against him, that if the permanently divorced woman comes with a child more than two years from divorce but less than six months from purchase, even if it is within his expression here, is not a problem because of what he presented earlier. The restriction to divorce is coincidental, because the ruling when he did not divorce her and bought her is the same, i.e., not for less than six months from it. So in establishing divorce in the first two pictures, it invalidates what was established with certainty, so it does not help. In return (رجعة) similarly, working contrary to the evidence indicating disapproval of return without uttering a statement. He said: (His lineage is established without a claim) because he is the child of his woman in 'iddah, not his slave woman, because it cannot be supposed that she conceived after the purchase, since his ownership of her does not make her permissible for him after severe prohibition until she marries another. Unlike if it were a light prohibition, where he divorced her after consummation with one irrevocable divorce, then when he buys her, it becomes permissible for him to have intercourse with her because she is in 'iddah from him and her 'iddah from him does not prohibit her to him. So if she gives birth more than six months, it is possible that it was after the purchase, so it is attributed to it because it is nearest, and the event is attributed to its nearest time, so it is the child of his slave woman and is not established without a claim. He said: (And in the second, two years or less) contradicts what he proceeded with earlier: the child of the irrevocably divorced woman is not established unless she comes with it within less than two years, so it should be the same here as some scholars said. And we have presented from al-Nahr the disagreement on that and that it is carried on variance of narration, so it is possible that
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the rulings on the duration of pregnancy and its implications for establishing lineage. The author begins by stating the agreed-upon Hanafi position that the maximum duration of pregnancy is two years, based on the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) that a child does not remain in the womb beyond two years, even for the length of a spindle's shadow—an expression of extreme brevity. The minimum is six months, derived from the Quranic verse: 'And his gestation and weaning are thirty months' [46:15], coupled with 'And his weaning is in two years' [31:14], leaving six months for gestation. The author then addresses a potential contradiction regarding Imam Abu Hanifa's view on breastfeeding, explaining that the thirty-month period is to be interpreted as the full span for both gestation and weaning, but the reduction occurs in gestation due to the hadith evidence. The central ruling is then presented: if a man marries a slave woman, divorces her, then purchases her, and she gives birth less than six months after the purchase, the child's lineage is established and he must acknowledge it; if she gives birth at six months or more, it is not established unless he makes a claim. This is because in the first case, the child is considered the offspring of the 'iddah period (conception before purchase), whereas in the second case, the child is attributed to the nearest possible conception time (after purchase), making the child the offspring of his owned slave woman, which requires a claim for lineage. The passage then discusses three subsidiary scenarios: (1) when a man tells his slave woman, 'If there is a child in your womb, it is from me,' and she testifies accordingly, requiring further evidence if contested; (2) conditional divorce tied to pregnancy, where if she gives birth within two years of the conditional statement, the divorce does not occur because the nearest conception time is not considered for rulings that would invalidate established principles; (3) a revocably divorced woman who gives birth within two years does not automatically effectuate a return (raj'a) because that would involve applying a ruling based on the nearest time that contradicts the intended legal consequence. The underlying principle is that events are attributed to the nearest possible time only when doing so does not invalidate what is established by definitive evidence or require abandoning the implied legal meaning (muqtada). The passage concludes by distinguishing between forms of divorce (before consummation, after consummation, with one or two pronouncements) and their effect on the time period for establishing lineage, noting the difference between 'light' and 'severe' prohibition (bainuna) in terms of considering the purchase time for the slave woman's child.

Key Terms
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; severs the marital bond completely, with no right of return except through a new marriage.
رجعة (raj'a): Return to marriage during the 'iddah period after a revocable divorce, by word or action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a man divorces his slave woman with one revocable divorce after consummation, then buys her, and she gives birth 10 months after the purchase, the child's lineage:
A) Is automatically established because it is within 2 years of divorce
B) Is automatically established because it is less than 2 years from purchase
C) Is not automatically established and requires a claim
D) Is not established at all because it exceeds 6 months from purchase

Key Takeaway
The six-month rule from purchase is the baseline, but exceptions exist for divorce before consummation (only time from divorce counts) and severe prohibition (only time from divorce, up to two years).

Talaq — Divorce

Paternity Duration after Divorce: The Bainuna Distinction
Explaining paternity time limits for divorced women and purchased slave-girls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for establishing paternity after divorce by distinguishing between light and severe bainuna, and identify the relevant time limits.

Core Idea
Paternity after irrevocable divorce extends to two years, but the time of purchase matters only in light bainuna.

The Hanafi school has detailed rules for when a child born after divorce is attributed to the ex-husband. The key is the type of divorce: revocable (raj'i) vs irrevocable (ba'in), and within irrevocable, light vs severe. This lesson unpacks those distinctions using the commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of divorce
Determine if the divorce was revocable or irrevocable. If revocable, paternity can be established even years later. If irrevocable, proceed to step 2.
Revocable divorce retains the marriage tie; 'iddah is still in effect.
2
Check for consummation and 'iddah
If irrevocable and consummated, she has an 'iddah and paternity extends to two years from divorce. If not consummated, she has no 'iddah and paternity only within six months from divorce.
This is the exception that corrects a possible misunderstanding.
3
Distinguish light vs severe bainuna
In light bainuna (single talaq after consummation), if the husband repurchases her, the time of purchase matters: birth within six months of purchase attributes paternity. In severe bainuna (two talaqs or three), even if purchased, paternity is established without claim up to two years from divorce, ignoring purchase time.
The severe prohibition blocks any possibility of intercourse after purchase.
4
Apply the time limits
For light bainuna: paternity established without claim if birth within two years of divorce AND within six months of purchase (if purchased). For severe bainuna: established without claim if within two years of divorce, regardless of purchase time.
If more than two years, only by explicit claim.

يثبت بلا دعوة قوله : ( وفي الثانية لسنتين فأقل مخالف لما مشى عليه فيما مر ولد معتدة البت لا يثبت إلا إذا أتت به لأقل من سنتين فينبغي أن يكون هنا كذلك كما قاله بعض الفضلاء، وقد قدمنا عن النهر الخلاف في (سابقاً) أي من قوله والبت لأقل منهما وإلا لا فإنه مصرح بأنها لو جاءت المبتوتة به لأكثر من سنتين من وقت الطلاق لا يثبت النسب فأطلقه هنا اعتماداً على ما قدمه قوله : ( وحكم معتدة لم تقر (الخ عبارة الفتح: وحكم معتدة عن بائن لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها ذلك اهـ. أي ثبوت النسب إلى سنتين بلا دعوة . قوله : (ولم يبين مفهومه قال في النهر : إنما لم يبينه استغناء بما مر من أن تأتي به لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار لا من وقت الشراء كما قال هنا . أنه مع الإقرار يشترط كحكم خصوص سبب المطلقة فإن ولدته لستة أشهر أو أكثر من وقت الشراء لا يلزمه وإلا لزمه، وتقييده في فتح القدير بالرجعي لا يفيد لأن البائن هنا كالرجعي إلا إذا كان غليظاً. والمراد من الشراء الملك أعم من أن يكون بشراء أو هبة أو إرث أو نحو ذلك لأن المفسد للنكاح الملك لا له. وأشار باقتصاره على الشراء إلى أنه لا فرق في هذا الحكم بين أن يعتقها بعد الشراء أو لا. وعند محمد يثبت النسب إلى سنتين بلا دعوة من يوم الشراء لأنه بالشراء بطل النكاح ووجبت العدة لكنها لا تظهر في حقه للملك وبالعتق ظهرت وحكم معتدة لم تقر بانقضاء عدتها كذلك، ولو لم يعتقها ولكن باعها فولدت لأكثر من ستة أشهر منذ باعها فعند أبي يوسف لا يثبت النسب وإن ادعاه إلا بتصديق المشتري لما مر أن النكاح بطل. وعند محمد يثبت بلا تصديق كما قال في العتق إلا أنه لا يثبت بلا دعوة لأن العدة ظهرت ثم ولم تظهر هنا. وقيد في فتح القدير حكم المسألة المذكورة في المختصر بما إذا اشتراها قبل أن تقر بانقضاء عدتها ولم يبين مفهومه قوله : ( ومن قال لأمته إن كان في بطنك ولد فهو مني فشهدت امرأة بالولادة فهي أم ولده لأن الحاجة إلى تعيين الولد ويثبت ذلك بشهادة القابلة بالإجماع، وقد ذكر في المختصر المرأة دون القابلة وكثيراً ما يذكرون القابلة والظاهر أن كونها القابلة ليس بشرط. أطلقه وقيدوه بأن تلده لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الإقرار وأن ولدته لستة أشهر أو أكثر لا يلزمه لاحتمال أنها حبلت بعد مقالة المولى فلم يكن المولى مدعياً هذا الولد بخلاف الأول لتيقننا بقيامه في البطن وقت القول فتيقناه بالدعوى، وما في غاية البيان من هذا إذا ولدته لأقل من ستة أشهر من وقت الطلاق سبق قلم إذ لا طلاق هنا لأن الكلام في الأمة المملوكة له، وإنما الاعتبار لوقت الإقرار ومثله لو قال إن كان في بطنك ولد فهو حر فولدت بعد ذلك لستة أشهر لم يعتق وإن ولدته لأقل منها عتق ولا فرق بين أن يقول في مسألة المختصر إن كان في بطنك ولد أو إن كان بها حبل فهو مني. وقيد بالتعليق لأنه لو قال هذه حامل مني يلزمه الولد وإن جاءت به لأكثر من ستة أشهر إلى سنتين حتى ينفيه كما في الغاية. قوله : ( ومن قال لغلام هو ابني ومات فقالت أمه أنا امرأته وهو ابنه يرثانه)
The chapter of divorce, section on establishing paternity. The issue: indeed, he is not obliged to accept the child unless she gives birth to the child less than six months from the time of divorce, if she gives birth at a full six months or more from the time of marriage. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: We have a consideration regarding it because divorce before consummation is irrevocable (ba'in), and the ruling for a separated woman is that the paternity of her child is established up to two years from the time of divorce. Yes, indeed Muhammad placed in al-Jami' al-Saghir regarding the one who was consummated with. End quote. And the answer is that this is the ruling of a separated woman when she is in 'iddah, and the one not consummated with has no 'iddah. As for the case where the divorce is two (talaqs), then the paternity of the child extends to two years from the time of divorce, even if he does not claim. If she gives birth after that, paternity is not established unless he claims it, because her prohibition is severe, so the conception is attributed to the furthest time, which is before the divorce, to presume rectitude between them. And it is mentioned in Ghayat al-Bayan that restricting this ruling to two talaqs creates a false impression, because one might think that if the divorce is a single irrevocable one, paternity is not established up to two years, but it is not so, because in irrevocable divorce, paternity is established up to two years from the time of divorce even without claim. End quote. And the answer is by distinguishing between light and severe irrevocability. In light irrevocability, the time of purchase is also considered, i.e., she gives birth less than six months from purchase, and if it is within two years from divorce. In severe irrevocability, that is not considered, such that even if she gives birth more than six months from purchase and within two years from divorce, his lineage is established without claim, so the difference becomes clear, and the false impression is in understanding the words of the scholars. The conclusion is that an exception is made from the ruling mentioned in al-Mukhtasar for the woman divorced before consummation and the one separated by two talaqs, for in these two cases, the time of purchase is not considered, only the time of divorce. In the first, the condition for establishing lineage is that she gives birth less than six months; in the second, two years or less. And it is known from what the author has previously stated that if this slave-girl's divorce were revocable (raj'i), then the lineage of her child is established even if she brings it after ten years or more after divorce. And if it were irrevocable, then she must bring it within two years or less, after ensuring it is six months from the time of purchase in both issues. So the objection does not apply if the irrevocably divorced woman brings it more than two years from divorce and less than six months from purchase, even if it falls under his expression here because of what he previously stated. And the restriction to divorce is agreed, because the ruling in the case where he did not divorce her and purchased her is likewise, i.e., not less than... He found the matter upon it, so in establishing divorce in the first two images, it negates what was established with certainty, so it does not help, and in the case of return (raj'a) likewise, the practice is contrary to the evidence that indicates dislike of return without speech. His statement: (His lineage is established without claim) because he is the child of his 'iddah, not his slave-girl, because it cannot be assumed that she conceived after purchase, since his ownership of her does not make her permissible for him after severe prohibition until she marries another, unlike the case of light prohibition, where he divorces her after consummation with a single irrevocable talaq, then if he purchases her, it is permissible for him to have intercourse with her because she is in his 'iddah and her 'iddah does not make her forbidden for him. So if she gives birth after more than six months, it is possible that it was after purchase, so it is attributed to him because it is closer, and the new event is attributed to its closest time, so it becomes the child of his slave-girl, so it is not established without claim. His statement: (And in the second, two years or less) contradicts what he proceeded with earlier, that the child of an irrevocably separated woman ('iddah) is not established unless she brings it within less than two years, so it should be similar here, as some scholars said. And we have previously mentioned from al-Nahr the difference of opinion on this, and that it is attributed to difference in narration, so it is possible that what is here is attributed to the other narration, consider it. His statement: (And if it is irrevocable, then it must...) i.e., light irrevocability, because he previously stated that in severe irrevocability, the time of purchase is not considered. His statement: (Because of what he previously stated) i.e., from his statement: and the irrevocable divorce (al-batt) is for less than both, otherwise no, for it clearly states that if the irrevocably divorced woman brings it more than two years from divorce, paternity is not established, so he generalized here relying on what he previously stated. His statement: (And the ruling of a woman in 'iddah who has not admitted...) the wording of al-Fath: and the ruling of a woman in 'iddah from an irrevocable divorce who has not admitted the expiry of her 'iddah is that, i.e., establishment of lineage up to two years without claim. His statement: (And he did not explain its implication). He said in al-Nahr: He only did not explain it because he relied on what passed, that she should bring it less than six months from the time of confession, not from the time of purchase, as stated here. That with confession, it is required like the ruling of the specific cause of the divorced woman: if she gives birth at six months or more from purchase, it is not binding on him, otherwise it is binding. And its restriction in Fath al-Qadir to revocable divorce is not beneficial, because the irrevocable here is like the revocable, unless it is severe. And what is meant by purchase is ownership, general, whether by purchase, gift, inheritance, or the like, because what invalidates the marriage is ownership, not speech. And by his restriction to purchase, he indicated that there is no difference in this ruling between whether he frees her after purchase or not. According to Muhammad, the lineage is established up to two years without claim from the day of purchase, because by purchase the marriage is nullified and 'iddah becomes obligatory, but it does not appear in his regard due to ownership, and by freeing it appears, and the ruling of a woman in 'iddah who has not admitted the expiry of her 'iddah is likewise. And if he did not free her but sold her, and she gave birth more than six months after he sold her, then according to Abu Yusuf, lineage is not established even if he claims it, except with the confirmation of the buyer, because, as passed, the marriage is nullified. And according to Muhammad, it is established without confirmation, as he said in the case of freeing, except that it is not established without claim, because the 'iddah appeared then, but here it did not appear. And in Fath al-Qadir, the ruling of the issue mentioned in al-Mukhtasar is restricted to when he purchased her before she admitted the expiry of her 'iddah, and he did not explain its implication. His statement: (And whoever says to his slave-girl, 'If in your womb there is a child, it is from me,' and a woman testified to the birth, then she is the mother of his child) because there is need to specify the child, and that is established by the testimony of the midwife by consensus. And in al-Mukhtasar, he mentioned the woman without the midwife, and they often mention the midwife, and apparently that she is the midwife is not a condition. He generalized it, and they restricted it that she gives birth less than six months from the time of confession, and if she gives birth at six months or more, it is not binding on him, because it is possible that she became pregnant after the master's statement, so the master was not claiming this child, unlike the first because we are certain of its existence in the womb at the time of the statement, so we are certain of his claim. And what is in Ghayat al-Bayan about this, that if she gives birth less than six months from the time of divorce, it is a slip of the pen, because there is no divorce here, as the speech is about a slave-girl owned by him, and the consideration is only for the time of confession. Similarly, if he said: 'If in your womb there is a child, it is free,' and she gives birth after six months, it does not become free, but if she gives birth less than that, it becomes free. And there is no difference between his saying in the issue of al-Mukhtasar: 'If in your womb there is a child' or 'If there is pregnancy in her, it is from me.' And he restricted it by the conditional, because if he said, 'This is pregnant from me,' the child becomes binding on him even if she brings it after more than six months up to two years, unless he repudiates it, as in al-Ghayah. His statement: (And whoever says to a boy, 'He is my son,' and dies, and his mother says, 'I am his wife and he is his son,' they both inherit from him.)
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addressing the rulings of establishing paternity (thubut al-nasab) in various scenarios involving divorce and purchase of a slave-girl. The commentary expands on the concise text of the matn, clarifying conditions and differences of opinion within the Hanafi school.

The passage begins by discussing a woman divorced before consummation. The key rule is that if she gives birth less than six months from the time of divorce, the child is attributed to the ex-husband. If she gives birth at six months or more from marriage, it could be from him, but if she gives birth at six months from divorce, it is not his because the minimum pregnancy is six months. The commentary from Ghayat al-Bayan raises an objection: since the woman is irrevocably divorced (ba'in) and has no 'iddah (because she was not consummated with), the general rule for an irrevocably divorced woman is that paternity extends up to two years from divorce. Why then restrict to six months? The answer is that the general rule applies only when the woman is in 'iddah. Since this woman has no 'iddah, the rule is different. So for a non-consummated marriage, the shortest possible pregnancy from the time of marriage determines paternity.

Then the passage discusses the case of a woman divorced with two talaqs (bainuna ghalizah). In this case, paternity extends to two years from divorce, even without a claim by the man, because the prohibition is severe. If she gives birth after two years, it is not established unless he explicitly claims paternity. The commentary clarifies that this might create a false impression that a single irrevocable divorce (bainuna khafifah) does not extend to two years. Actually, in that case too, paternity extends to two years, but the difference is that in light irrevocability, the time of purchase (if he later buys her) is also considered: if she gives birth less than six months from purchase, it is his; if more, it is not without claim. In severe irrevocability, even if she gives birth more than six months from purchase, paternity is established without claim, because she remains forbidden to him after purchase until she marries another.

Next, the commentary addresses the ruling for a revocably divorced woman: paternity is established even if she gives birth after ten years or more from divorce, because the marriage tie persists. For an irrevocably divorced woman (light), she must give birth within two years from divorce and also at least six months from purchase (if bought) for paternity to be established without claim. The author of the commentary notes that this might seem contradictory to an earlier statement, but it is resolved by considering different types of bainuna.

The passage then moves to the case of a man who purchases his divorced slave-girl. If she gives birth less than six months from purchase, the child is his; if six months or more, it is not. This is because the purchase nullifies the marriage, and she goes into 'iddah. According to Imam Muhammad, once the male buys her, if she later gives birth within two years from purchase, the child is his without claim, because the purchase nullifies the marriage and 'iddah is obligatory, but it is not apparent due to ownership. Upon freeing her, the 'iddah becomes apparent. There is a difference between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the case where he sells her instead of freeing. If she gives birth more than six months after sale, Abu Yusuf says lineage is not established even with claim unless the buyer confirms, while Muhammad says it is established without confirmation but requires claim.

The final part discusses the case of a man who says to his slave-girl: 'If there is a child in your womb, it is from me.' If she gives birth less than six months from that statement, the child is established as his and she becomes an umm walad. This is based on the testimony of a single woman (the midwife) by consensus. If she gives birth after six months or more, it is not his, because she could have conceived after the statement. The same applies if he says 'the child is free' – it is free only if born within six months. However, if he simply says 'She is pregnant from me' without a conditional, the child is his even up to two years unless he repudiates it.

Finally, the passage mentions the case of a man who says to a boy 'He is my son' and dies, and the boy's mother claims to be his wife. Both inherit from him if the claim is accepted. This is a brief comment without full elaboration.

Throughout, the commentary demonstrates the Hanafi method of distinguishing cases based on the type of separation (bainuna khafifah vs. ghalizah, raj'i vs. ba'in) and the timing of birth relative to key events (marriage, divorce, purchase). It highlights the importance of certainty in paternity and the legal principles of attributing events to the nearest possible time.

Key Terms
ثبوت النسب (thubut al-nasab): Legal establishment of paternity of a child to a specific father.
البائن بينونة خفيفة (al-ba'in baynuna khafifah): Irrevocable divorce with a degree that allows remarriage without intermediate marriage, such as a single talaq before consummation or after consummation.
البائن بينونة غليظة (al-ba'in baynuna ghalizah): Irrevocable divorce with severe prohibition, requiring an intermediate marriage (tahlil) to remarry, e.g., three talaqs or two talaqs after consummation.
العدة (al-'iddah): Waiting period after divorce or death of husband before a woman can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A woman is divorced with two talaqs after consummation (severe bainuna). Her ex-husband purchases her a year later, and she gives birth 18 months after purchase (30 months after divorce). Is paternity established without a claim?
A) Yes, because birth is within two years of purchase.
B) No, because birth is more than two years after divorce.
C) Yes, because severe bainuna ignores purchase time and birth is within two years of divorce.
D) No, because birth is more than six months after purchase.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between light and severe bainuna, with purchase time affecting paternity only in light bainuna, and the default rule that paternity is established without claim for children born within two years of an irrevocable divorce (with the exception of non-consummated marriages).

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Inheritance When Father Acknowledges Son and Mother Claims Marriage
Ruling on inheritance via paternity acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi istihsan to determine when a mother inherits based on paternity acknowledgment and distinguish it from the qiyas counterargument.

Core Idea
When a free mother is known, acknowledging paternity implies valid marriage — custom overrides qiyas.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about inheritance when a father acknowledges a son but no explicit marriage is proven. The key question is whether the mother and son inherit. The preferred view (istihsan) says yes under specific conditions, overriding the analogical reasoning (qiyas) that would deny it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The text presents two different factual scenarios: (1) the mother is known to be free and the boy's mother, and (2) the mother's freedom is unknown or the heir claims she is an umm walad. The ruling differs between them.
2
Analyze the qiyas argument
The qiyas argues that since paternity can be established through various means (valid marriage, invalid marriage, intercourse with uncertainty, concubinage), acknowledging paternity alone does not prove valid marriage, so inheritance should be denied in all cases.
3
Understand the istihsan reasoning
Istihsan relies on the strong custom (urf) that children are usually born from a valid marriage. When the mother is known free and mother of the son, the strong apparent (al-zahir al-qawi) is that a valid marriage existed, so inheritance is granted.
4
Apply the ruling to each scenario
First scenario: mother known free → inheritance. Second scenario: freedom unknown or heir claims umm walad → no inheritance. The latter is because apparent freedom based on abode repels slavery but does not establish inheritance rights.
Also note: the text discusses whether a mahr mithl is due, and concludes it is not.
5
Consider the implications for fatwa
The mufta bihi is the istihsan position. A mufti must verify the mother's status and the strength of the evidence before issuing a ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امرأة بالولادة فهي أم ولده ومن قال لغلام هو ابني ومات فقالت أمه أنا امرأته وهو ابنه يرثانه وإن جهلت حريتها فقال وارثه أنت أم ولد أبي فلا ميراث لها . والقياس أن لا ميراث لها لأن النسب كما يثبت بالنكاح الصحيح يثبت بالنكاح الفاسد وبالوطء عن شبهته وبملك اليمين فلم يكن قوله إقراراً بالنكاح وجه الاستحسان أن المسألة فيما إذا كانت معروفة بالحرية وبكونها أم الغلام، والنكاح الصحيح المتعين لذلك وضعاً هو وعادة لأنه الموضوع لحصول الأولاد دون غيره فهما احتمالان لا يعتبران في مقابلة الظاهر القوي، وكذا احتمال كونه طلقها في صحته وانقضت عدتها لأنه لما ثبت النكاح وجب الحكم بقيامه ما لم يتحقق زواله. فإن قيل : إن النكاح يثبت بمقتضى ثبوت النسب وهو لا عموم له فيتقدر بقدر الحاجة قلنا : النكاح غير متنوع إلى نكاح موجب للإرث والنسب وإلى غير موجب لهما، فإذا تعين النكاح الصحيح لزم .بلوازمه. وفي غاية البيان: إنه ليس من الاقتضاء في شيء لأن المقتضي وهو النسب يصح بلا ثبوت المقتضي وهو النكاح بأن يكون الوطء عن شبهة أو تكون أم ولده فلم يفتقر ثبوت النسب إلى النكاح لا محالة قوله : (وإن جهلت حريتها فقال وارثة أنت أم ولد أبي فلا ميراث لها لأن ظهور الحرية باعتبار الدار حجة في دفع الرق لا في استحقاق الإرث وتقييده بقول الوارث اتفاقي لأن الجهل بحريتها كاف لعدم ميراثها قال الوارث أنت أم ولد أبي أو لم يقل كما أطلقه في غاية البيان معللاً بأن للوارث أن يقول ذلك، ولعل فائدته أن الوارث لو كان صغيراً فإنه لا ميراث لها أيضاً وإن لم يقل شيئاً. ولم يذكر المصنف رحمه الله أن لها مهراً تعند إقرار الوارث أنها أم ولد أبيه. وذكر التمرتاشي أن لها مهر مثلها لأنهم أقروا بالدخول ولم يثبت كونها أم ولد بقولهم، ورده في غاية البيان بأن الدخول إنما يوجب مهر المثل في غير صورة النكاح إذا كان الوطء عن شبهة ولم يثبت النكاح هنا والأصل عدم الشبهة فبأي دليل يحمل على ذلك فلا يجب مهر المثل. وأيضاً إنما لم نوجب الإرث لأن الاستصحاب لا يصلح للإثبات فلو وجب مهر المثل لكان صالحاً للإثبات فلا يجوز .اهـ والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب.
A woman who gives birth is his um walad. And whoever says to a boy 'he is my son' and dies, and his mother says 'I am his wife and he is his son', they inherit him. And if her freedom is unknown and the heir says 'you are the um walad of my father', then she has no inheritance. The analogy is that she has no inheritance because lineage is established by valid marriage, invalid marriage, intercourse with uncertainty, and by right hand possession. So his statement is not an acknowledgment of marriage. The istihsan is that the case is when she is known to be free and to be the boy's mother, and valid marriage is what is specifically established for that by rule and custom, because it is the subject for having children without other possibilities. So these are two probabilities that are not considered against the strong apparent. Similarly, the probability that he divorced her during good health and her 'iddah ended, because when marriage is established, it must be judged to continue as long as its removal is not verified. If it is said: 'Marriage is established by virtue of the establishment of lineage, and lineage has no generality, so it is limited to the extent of need,' we reply: Marriage is not divided into marriage that causes inheritance and lineage and marriage that does not cause them. So when valid marriage is established, its consequences follow. And in Ghayat al-Bayan: It is not a matter of implication at all, because the implicant, which is lineage, can be valid without establishing the implication, which is marriage, if intercourse occurs through uncertainty or if she is his um walad. So establishing lineage does not necessarily require marriage. His statement: (And if her freedom is unknown and the heir says 'you are the um walad of my father', then she has no inheritance because the apparent freedom based on abode is evidence to repel slavery, not to establish entitlement to inheritance. And its restriction to the heir's statement is incidental, because ignorance of her freedom is sufficient for her lack of inheritance, whether the heir says 'you are the um walad of my father' or not, as he absolved in Ghayat al-Bayan, with the reasoning that the heir can say that. Perhaps its benefit is that if the heir is a minor, she also has no inheritance even if he says nothing. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not mention that she has a dowry upon the heir's acknowledgment that she is the um walad of his father. Al-Tamartashi mentioned that she has a mahr mithl because they admitted to entry but her being um walad is not established by their statement. But it was refuted in Ghayat al-Bayan: Entry only necessitates mahr mithl in the absence of marriage if intercourse is by uncertainty, but marriage is not established here, and the original is absence of uncertainty, so by what evidence is it carried to that? Thus mahr mithl is not obligatory. Also, we did not obligate inheritance because istishab is not valid for establishment; if mahr mithl were obligatory, it would be valid for establishment, so it is not permissible. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding inheritance when a man acknowledges a boy as his son and dies. The ruling is that the mother and son inherit if she is known as free and the boy's mother, because the acknowledgment implies a valid marriage. The opponent's argument (qiyas) is that paternity can be established through various means (invalid marriage, intercourse with uncertainty, concubinage), so acknowledgment alone does not prove a valid marriage warranting inheritance. The stronger view (istihsan) relies on the strong presumption that a known free woman and mother must have been in a valid marriage, since that is the usual cause for children. The author then addresses a second scenario: if the mother's freedom is unknown and the heir says she is a concubine (um walad of the father), she gets no inheritance because freedom based on abode only repels slavery but does not establish inheritance rights. There is also a discussion on whether she is entitled to mahr mithl; the preferred view is no, because the heir's statement does not prove she is um walad, and istishab cannot establish a right. Throughout, the author defends the Hanafi position that valid marriage is inferred from paternity acknowledgment when supported by strong circumstantial evidence.

Key Terms
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has borne a child to her master.
الاستحسان (istihsan): A method of reasoning that prefers a ruling based on stronger evidence of custom or necessity over an analogical deduction.
القياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction from a known ruling to a new case based on shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first scenario described, what is the condition for the mother to inherit along with the son?
A) She must be known as free and the mother of the boy.
B) She must have a written marriage contract.
C) The heir must acknowledge her as a wife.
D) She must be a free woman and the child must be a boy.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school infers valid marriage from paternity acknowledgment when the mother is known free and mother of the child, overriding the general qiyas that paternity can arise without marriage.

The Definition and Nature of Custody (Hadanah)
Definition and conditions for custodians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi definition of ḥaḍānah, distinguish it from financial guardianship, and list the conditions for a female custodian.

Core Idea
Custody is the child's right, grounded in compassion; women are preferred for physical care, but the father is responsible for expenses.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq begins the chapter on custody. It provides linguistic roots, defines custody as a right of the minor, and divides responsibilities between physical care (assigned to women due to compassion) and financial guardianship (assigned to men). The text also enumerates conditions for the custodian.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic root
Find the Arabic word 'ḥaḍānah' and its meanings related to nurturing and caring.
The root also appears in bird nesting.
2
Understand the twofold nature of child welfare
Distinguish between physical custody (bodily care) and financial guardianship (property management).
Each is assigned to those most capable.
3
Explain why women are preferred for physical custody
Women have greater compassion (shafaqah) and stay at home, making them more suitable.
This is a rationale (ta‘līl).
4
List the conditions for a female custodian
From the text: free (or mukātabah if she gives birth to a similar child), adult, sane, trustworthy, capable, free from a foreign husband, and free from hatred toward the child.
Additional conditions from commentary include not being an apostate.
5
Note conditions for a male custodian
The same conditions apply except the last (freedom from a foreign husband is irrelevant for men).
This is mentioned briefly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الحضانة بيان لمن يحضن الولد الذي ثبت نسبه وهي بكسر الحاء وفتحها تربية الولد، والحاضنة المرأة توكل بالصبي فترفعه وتربيه وقد حضنت ولدها حضانة من باب طلب، وحضن الطائر بيضه حضناً إذا جثم عليه بكنفه يحضنه؛ كذا في المغرب، وفي ضياء الحلوم: حضنت المرأة ولدها حضانة وحضنت الحمامة بيضها حضوناً أي جعلته في حضنها، وحضنه عن حاجته أي حبسه، وحضنه عن الأمر إذا انحاه عنه، والحضن ما دون الإبط . ثم اعلم أن الحضانة حق الصغير لاحتياجه إلى من يمسكه فتارة يحتاج إلى من يقوم بمنفعة بدنه في حضانته وتارة إلى من يقوم بما له حتى لا يلحقه الضرر وجعل كل واحد منهما إلى من أقوم به وأبصر، فالولاية في المال جعلت إلى الأب والجد لأنهم أبصر وأقوم في التجارة من النساء، وحق الحضانة جعل إلى النساء لأنه أبصر وأقوم على حفظ الصبيان من الرجال لزيادة شفقتهن وملازمتهن للبيوت، واتفقوا على أن الأب يجبر على نفقته مطلقاً ويجب عليه إمساكه وحفظه وصيانته إذا استغنى عن النساء لأن ذلك حق للصغير عليه واختلفوا في وجوب حضانته على الأم ونحوها من النساء وفي جبرها إذا امتنعت؛ فصرح في الهداية بأنها لا تجبر لأنها أن تعجز الحضانة عن وصححه في التبيين وفي الولوالجية وعليه الفتوى، وفي الواقعات والفتوى على عدم الجبر لوجهين: أحدهما أنها ربما لا تقدر على الحضانة، والثاني أن الحضانة حق الأم والمولى ولا يجبر على استيفاء حقه .اهـ. وفي الخلاصة وقال مشايخنا : ولا تجبر وأن تخلو من زوج أجنبي. وإن كان الحاضن ذكراً فشرطه أن يكون كذلك ما عدا الأخير. وهذا قلته منفرداً به أخذاً من كلامهم ولم أر أحداً ذكر هذه الشروط على هذه الكيفية على علمي الآن والله تعالى هو الموفق ا هـ . قلت : وينبغي أن يزيد بعد قوله حرة أو مكاتبة لو ولدها مثلها لأن المكاتبة إذا ولدت في الكتابة فحضانته لها كما سيأتي. وأن يزيد بعد قوله وأن تخلو من زوج أجنبي «أو مبغض للولد» كما سيأتي عن القنية تأمل. وينبغي أن يزيد في الشروط وعدم ردتها إلا أن يقال يغني عنه قوله قادرة لأنها تحبس وتضرب. قوله : (ثم اعلم أن الحضانة حق الصغير الخ ) قال في النهر : وهل هي حق من تثبت لها الحضانة أو حق الولد؟ خلاف قيل بالأول فلا تجبر إن هي امتنعت ورجحه غير واحد، وفي الواقعات وغيرها وعليه الفتوى وفي الخلاصة قال :مشايخنا لا تجبر الأم عليها وكذلك الخالة إذا لم يكن زوج لأنها ربما تعجز عن ذلك. وقيل بالثاني فتجبر واختاره أبو الليث وخواهر زاده والهندواني، وأيده في الفتح بما في الحاكم لو اختلعت على أن تترك ولدها عند الزوج فالخلع جائز والشرط باطل لأنه حق الولد. فأفاد أن قول الفقهاء جواب ظاهر الرواية ثم قال في الفتح : فإن لم يوجد غيرها أجبرت بلا خلاف ا هـ . وعلى هذا فما في الظهيرية قالت الأم لا حاجة لي به وقالت الجدة أنا آخذه دفع إليها لأن الحضانة حقها فإذا أسقطت حقها صح الإسقاط منها، لكن إنما يكون لها ذلك إذا كان للولد ذو رحم محرم كما هنا، أما إذا لم يكن أجبرت على الحضانة كيلا يضيع الولد،
Chapter of Custody. Explanation of who has custody of the child whose lineage is established. 'Hadanah' (with kasr or fatha of ha) is the upbringing of the child. The custodian (hāḍinah) is the woman entrusted with the boy to raise and nurture him. She has performed custody (hiḍānah) of her child, from the verb ṭalaba. The bird cuddles its eggs. Thus in al-Mughrib. In Ḍiyāʼ al-Ḥulūm: The woman performed custody of her child, the pigeon cuddled its eggs, i.e., she placed it in her lap. He prevented him from his need, i.e., withheld him. He turned him away from the matter. The lap is what is below the armpit. Then know that custody is the right of the minor because of his need for someone to hold him. Sometimes he needs someone to perform the benefit of his body in his custody, and sometimes someone to manage his property so that no harm befalls him. Each of these is given to the one most capable and knowledgeable. Guardianship of property is given to the father and grandfather because they are more knowledgeable and capable in trade than women. The right of custody is given to women because they are more knowledgeable and capable in protecting children than men, due to their greater compassion and their staying in homes. They agreed that the father is compelled to spend for the child absolutely, and it is obligatory on him to hold, protect, and maintain the child when the child does not need women, because that is a right of the minor upon him. And they differed regarding the obligation of custody upon the mother and her like among women, and regarding compelling her if she refuses. It is explicitly stated in al-Hidayah that she is not compelled because custody may be beyond her; this was declared correct in al-Tabyīn and al-Walwālijiyyah, and the fatwa is upon it. In al-Wāqi‘āt, the fatwa is against compulsion for two reasons: one, she may not be able to handle custody; two, custody is the right of the mother and the guardian, and one is not compelled to enforce one's own right. In al-Khulāṣah: Our scholars said: She is not compelled... and that she be free from a foreign husband. If the custodian is male, his condition is that he be like that except the last. I said this alone, taking from their words, and I have not seen anyone mention these conditions in this way to my knowledge now. And Allah is the Grantor of success. I said: It is appropriate to add after his statement 'free' or 'mukātabah' if she gives birth to a child like herself because the mukātabah, if she gives birth while in kitābah, her custody belongs to him as will come. And to add after his statement 'and that she be free from a foreign husband' 'or someone who hates the child' as will come from al-Qunyah. And it is appropriate to add among the conditions 'absence of apostasy' unless it is said that his statement 'capable' suffices because she is imprisoned and beaten. His statement: (Then know that custody is the right of the minor...) In al-Nahr: Is it the right of the one who has custody or the right of the child? There is a difference of opinion. Some said the first, so she is not compelled if she refuses; many preferred this. In al-Wāqi‘āt and others, the fatwa is upon this. In al-Khulāṣah: Our scholars said: The mother is not compelled to custody, nor is the maternal aunt if she has no husband, because she may be unable to do so. Some said the second, so she is compelled; this was chosen by Abu al-Layth, Khwāharzādah, and al-Hinduwānī. And it was supported in al-Fatḥ by what is in al-Ḥākim: If she takes khul‘ on the condition that she leave her child with the husband, the khul‘ is valid and the condition is void because it is the right of the child. So it shows that the jurists' statement is the answer of the apparent narration. Then he said in al-Fatḥ: If there is no one else besides her, she is compelled without dispute. And accordingly, what is in al-Ẓāhiriyyah: The mother said 'I have no need of him' and the grandmother said 'I will take him', the child is given to her because custody is her right; if she drops her right, the dropping is valid. But she has that only if the child has a mahram relative as here; if not, she is compelled to custody so that the child is not lost.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on custody (ḥaḍānah) in al-Bahr al-Rā’iq. It begins with linguistic definitions: ḥaḍānah is the nurturing of a child; the ḥāḍinah is the entrusted woman. The author then establishes the foundational principle: custody is the right of the child, not of the adult, because the child needs care. The care is twofold: physical (bodily benefit) and financial (property management). Physical custody is given to women because they are more compassionate and stay at home, while financial guardianship is given to men (father, grandfather) as they are more knowledgeable in trade. The text reports consensus that the father must provide maintenance and care when the child is independent of women. However, there is disagreement on whether the mother can be compelled to provide custody. The preferred view (muftā bihī) is that she cannot be compelled, for two reasons: she may lack ability, and custody is her right. The minority view (Abu al-Layth, etc.) holds she is compelled because it is the child's right. The text also details conditions for a female custodian: she must be free, adult, sane, trustworthy, capable, and free from a foreign husband and from anyone who hates the child (added by the commentator). For male custodians, similar conditions apply except the last. The passage concludes with a note that if no other custodian exists, the mother is compelled without dispute, to prevent the child's loss.

Key Terms
الحضانة (al-ḥaḍānah): The legal right and duty to nurture and physically care for a minor child.
الجبر (al-jabr): Legal compulsion to perform an act, here specifically compelling the mother to take custody.
الشفقة (al-shafaqah): Compassion or tenderness, considered the basis for women's suitability for custody.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are women preferred over men for physical custody (ḥaḍānah)?
A) Women are legally required to take custody.
B) Women have greater compassion and stay at home.
C) Men are too busy with trade.
D) Women are stronger in raising children.

Key Takeaway
Custody (ḥaḍānah) is the child's right, not the adult's, and women are preferred custodians due to their compassion, provided they meet specific conditions.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
