Custody of a Girl: From Mother to Father Based on Maturity
Rulings on custody of a girl from mother/grandmother to father

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on custody of a girl and explain when the father takes over according to the mufta bihi opinion, including the effect of corruption of the times.

Core Idea
A girl's custody shifts to father when she reaches the threshold of desire, not necessarily at menstruation, due to societal change.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rules on who is entitled to custody of a young girl and at what point custody transfers from the mother or grandmother to the father. While the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah) holds that the mother retains custody until menstruation, the later fatwa, due to corruption of the times, transfers custody when the girl becomes desirable (mushtahah). We will walk through the reasoning, the intra-madhhab difference, and how the operative ruling is applied today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule and its reasoning
The mother and grandmother have priority for custody until the girl menstruates. The reasoning is that before puberty she needs to learn feminine etiquette, which the mother is better suited to teach; after puberty she needs protection, for which the father is stronger.
The text notes that if the author had said 'until she reaches puberty' it would be clearer.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute and the basis for change
Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf hold that due to corruption of the times (fasad al-zaman), the girl should be handed to the father when she reaches the threshold of desire (shahwa), even before menstruation. This is because the need for protection arises earlier due to societal decay.
Abu al-Layth estimated this at nine years; others at eleven. The fatwa is on the view that it is when she becomes desirable.
3
Apply the mufta bihi criterion
The relied-upon fatwa is that the father takes custody when the girl reaches the threshold of desire, not necessarily at menstruation. This overrides the apparent narration.
The text cites al-Tabyin and others that the fatwa is given on this view in our time.
4
Note exceptions and procedural points
Enslaved women and umm al-walad have no right to custody. If the girl is married before puberty and is not yet desirable, the mother's custody does not fall. If parents dispute whether she has menstruated, the mother's word is taken.
The text also discusses cases where the grandmother claims custody and the father disputes, with detailed procedural rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وبها حتى تحيض) أي الأم والجدة أحق بالصغيرة حتى تحيض لأن بعد الاستغناء تحتاج إلى معرفة آداب النساء والمرأة على ذلك أقدر، وبعد البلوغ تحتاج إلى التحصين والحفظ والأب فيه أقوى وأهدى وبه علم أنه لو قال حتى تبلغ لكان أولى. وعن محمد أنها تدفع إلى الأب إذا بلغت حد الشهوة لتحقق الحاجة إلى الصيانة قال في النقاية : وهو المعتبر لفساد الزمان . وفي نفقات الخصاف : وعن أبي يوسف مثله. وفي التبيين : وبه يفتى في زماننا والأخ لأب أحق من الخال لأنه عصبة وأقرب قوله : ( يدفع إلى الأخ لأم الخ) ذكر في الفتاوى الهندية أن أبا الأم أولى من الأخ لأم والخال .
وقدر بسبع وبها حتى تحيض وغيرهما أحق بها حتى تشتهي ولا حق للأمة وأم الولد ما لكثرة الفساد. وفي الخلاصة وغياث المفتي: والاعتماد على هذه الروايات لفساد الزمان . فالحاصل أن الفتوى على خلاف ظاهر الرواية فقد صرح في التجنيس بأن ظاهر الرواية أنها أحق بها حتى تحيض واختلف في حد الشهوة. وفي الولوالجية: وليس لها حد مقدر لأنه يختلف باختلاف حال المرأة. وفي التبيين وغيره وبنت إحدى عشرة سنة مشتهاة في قولهم جميعاً. وقدره أبو الليث بتسع سنين وعليه الفتوى ا هـ . وأشار المصنف إلى أنها لو زوجت قبل أن تبلغ لا تسقط حضانتها . وقال في القنية : الصغيرة إذا لم تكن مشتهاة ولها زوج يسقط حق الأم في حضانتها ما دامت لا تصلح للرجال إلا في رواية عن أبي يوسف إذا كانت يستأنس بها ا هـ . وظاهره أنها إذا صلحت للرجال قبل البلوغ وقد زوجها أبوها فإنه لا حضانة لأمها اتفاقاً فيحتاج إطلاق المختصر إلى تقييد نعم على المفتى به فهو ظاهر ولم أر حكم ما إذا اختلف الأب والأم في حيضها فقالت الأم لم تحض وقال الأب حاضت أو في البلوغ بالسن، وينبغي أن يكون القول قول الأم كما لو ادعى تزوجها وأنكرت بجامع أنه يدعي سقوط حقها وهي تنكر قوله : (وغيرهما أحق بها حتى تشتهي) أي غير الأم والجدة أحق بالصغيرة حتى تشتهي فيأخذها الأب. وفي الجامع الصغير : حتى تستغني لأنها لا تقدر على استخدامها ولهذا لا تؤجرها للخدمة فلا يحصل المقصود بخلاف الأم والجدة لقدرتهما عليه شرعاً. وأطلق في الجدة فشمل جدته من أمه ومن أبيه كما في فتح القدير. وفي الظهيرية : ولو أن امرأة جاءت بالصبي تطلب النفقة من أبيه فقالت هذا ابن ابنتي منك وقد ماتت أمه فأعطني نفقته فقال الأب صدقت هذا ابني من ابنتك فأما أمه فلم تمت وهي في منزلي وأراد أخذ الصبي منها لم يكن له ذلك حتى يعلم القاضي أمه وتحضر هي فتأخذه لأنه لما أقر أنها جدة الصبي فقد أقر أن لها حق الحضانة ثم يدعي قيام من هو أولى منها وذا محتمل، فإن أحضر الأب امرأة فقال هذه ابنتك وهذا ابني منها وقالت الجدة ما هذه ابنتي وقد ماتت ابنتي أم هذا الصبي فالقول في هذا قول الرجل والمرأة التي . ويدفع الصبي إليه لأن الفراش لهما فيكون الولد لهما، وصار هذا كالزوجين إذا كان بينهما ولد فقالت المرأة هو ابني من زوج آخر وقال الرجل هو ابني من امرأة أخرى فإنه يحكم بكونه ابناً لهما لأن الفراش لهما فيكون الولد لهما، وكذلك الجدة لو حضرت وقالت هذا ابن ابنتي من هذا الرجل وقد ماتت أمه فقال الرجل هذا ابني من غير ابنتك من امرأة لي فالقول قوله ويأخذ الصبي منها، ولو أحضر الرجل امرأة وقال هذا ابني من هذه لا من ابنتك وقالت الجدة ما هذه أمه بل أمه ابنتي وقالت التي أحضرها الرجل صدقت ما أنا بأمه وقد كذب هذا الرجل ولكني امرأته فإن الأب أولى به فيأخذه. وعلل الخصاف رحمه الله الكتاب فقال : لأنه لما قال هذا ابني من هذه المرأة فقد أنكر كونها جدة له فيكون منكراً الحق لها في الحضانة أصلاً وهي أقرت له بالحق ا هـ
His statement: (And with her until she menstruates) meaning the mother and grandmother have more right to the young girl until she menstruates, because after independence she needs to learn women's etiquette and the woman is more capable of that, and after puberty she needs protection and preservation, and the father is stronger and more guiding in that. And by this it is known that if he said 'until she reaches puberty' it would be better. And from Muhammad: she is handed over to the father when she reaches the threshold of desire because the need for protection is realized. He said in al-Niqayah: It is considered due to the corruption of the times. And in Nafaqat al-Khassaf: from Abu Yusuf similar. And in al-Tabyin: And by this fatwa is given in our time. The paternal brother has more right than the maternal uncle because he is an agnate and closer. His statement: (He is handed over to the maternal brother etc.) It is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyyah that the maternal grandfather has more right than the maternal brother and maternal uncle. And it is estimated by seven and with her until she menstruates, and others have more right to her until she desires, and there is no right for an enslaved woman or umm walad due to much corruption. And in al-Khulasah and Ghayat al-Mufti: Reliance is on these narrations because of the corruption of the times. So the conclusion is that the fatwa is contrary to the apparent narration, for it is stated in al-Tajn is that the apparent narration is that she has more right until she menstruates, and they differed on the limit of desire. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: There is no fixed limit for it because it differs according to the woman's situation. And in al-Tabyin and others: A girl of eleven years is considered desirable in all their statements. And Abu al-Layth estimated it at nine years, and upon that is the fatwa. End. And the author indicated that if she is married before she reaches puberty, her custody is not nullified. And he said in al-Qunyah: A young girl if she is not desirable and has a husband, the right of the mother in her custody falls as long as she is not suitable for men, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf if she is taken pleasure in. End. And its apparent meaning is that if she becomes suitable for men before puberty and her father has married her, then there is no custody for her mother by agreement, so the absolute statement of al-Mukhtar needs qualification, yes upon the mufta bihi it is apparent. And I have not seen the ruling if the father and mother differ on her menstruation: the mother says she has not menstruated and the father says she has, or on puberty by age. And it should be that the mother's statement is taken as in the case where he claims she married him and she denies, because he claims the fall of her right while she denies. His statement: (And others have more right to her until she desires) meaning other than the mother and grandmother have more right to the young girl until she desires, then the father takes her. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: until she becomes independent because she cannot employ her, and for this she is not hired out for service, so the purpose is not achieved, unlike the mother and grandmother because they are able to do so legally. And he released the term grandmother, so it includes his grandmother from his mother and from his father, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Zahiriyyah: And if a woman comes with a boy seeking maintenance from his father and says, 'This is my daughter's son from you and his mother has died, so give me his maintenance,' and the father says, 'You have spoken the truth, this is my son from your daughter, but his mother has not died and she is in my home,' and he wants to take the boy from her, he does not have that until the judge knows his mother and she comes and takes him, because when he acknowledged that she is the grandmother of the boy, he acknowledged that she has a right to custody, then he claims the existence of one who has more right than her, and this is possible. Then if the father brings a woman and says, 'This is your daughter and this is my son from her,' and the grandmother says, 'This is not my daughter, and my daughter who is the mother of this boy has died,' then the statement in this is the statement of the man and the woman who... and the boy is handed over to him because the bed belongs to them, so the child belongs to them. And this becomes like the case of a married couple with a child: the woman says, 'He is my son from another husband,' and the man says, 'He is my son from another woman,' then it is judged that he is a son of both because the bed belongs to them, so the child belongs to them. Similarly, if the grandmother comes and says, 'This is my daughter's son from this man and his mother has died,' and the man says, 'This is my son from other than your daughter, from my wife,' then the statement is his and he takes the boy from her. And if the man brings a woman and says, 'This is my son from this woman, not from your daughter,' and the grandmother says, 'This is not his mother, rather his mother is my daughter,' and the woman brought by the man says, 'You have spoken the truth, I am not his mother, and this man has lied, but I am his wife,' then the father has more right to him and takes him. And al-Khassaf, may Allah have mercy on him, commented on the book saying: Because when he said, 'This is my son from this woman,' he has denied that she is his grandmother, so he is denying her right to custody completely, while she has acknowledged his right.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage covers the custody (hadanah) of a girl. First, the mother and grandmother have the strongest right to the girl until she menstruates. The reasoning is that after basic independence, she needs to learn feminine etiquette, which the mother is better at teaching. After menstruation (puberty), she needs protection and guarding, for which the father is stronger. However, due to corruption of the times, the fatwa according to Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf is that the girl is handed to the father when she reaches the threshold of sexual desire (shahwa), which may occur before menstruation. The text then states that others (like paternal brother) have more right than maternal uncle. Then a summary: 'estimated by seven' (referring to the boy) and 'with her until menstruation' and 'others have more right to her until she desires'. Enslaved women and umm walad have no right to custody. The fatwa on the girl's age for transfer to father is disputed: some say until menstruation (apparent narration), but due to corruption, the fatwa is that she is handed over when she becomes desirable, which is estimated at eleven years by some, nine years by Abu al-Layth. Marriage before puberty does not end the mother's custody if the girl is not yet desirable. If parents differ on whether she has menstruated or reached the age of desire, the mother's word is taken because she is denying the fall of her right. The text then discusses procedural cases involving conflicting claims of grandmother and father over a boy.

Key Terms
شهوة (shahwah): Sexual desire; the threshold at which a girl becomes desirable and needs protection from potential fitna.
مشتهاة (mushtahah): A girl who has reached the age of sexual attractiveness, at which point she is considered desirable to men.
أمة (amah): An enslaved woman who is owned as a slave.
أم الولد (umm al-walad): A slave concubine who has borne a child to her master, thus gaining future freedom upon his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi opinion due to corruption of the times, when is a girl handed over from her mother/grandmother to her father?
A) When she menstruates
B) When she reaches the age of seven
C) When she reaches the threshold of sexual desire (shahwa)
D) When she attains the age of majority at eighteen

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi on custody of a girl prioritizes protection when she becomes desirable, even before menstruation, due to changing societal conditions.

Custody Duration: Mother's Right Until Menstruation or Desire?
Commentary on the mother's custody duration and transition to father when girl desires men

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the mufta bihi Hanafi rule on when a mother's custody ends and apply it to determine the father's entitlement.

Core Idea
The mother's custody ends when the girl becomes 'desired' (shahwah), not at a fixed age or menstruation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the duration of the mother's right to custody. While the apparent narration sets the limit at menstruation, the relied-upon fatwa shifts it to the onset of desire (shahwah) due to the corruption of time. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining when the father becomes entitled to custody.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
State the ruling from the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah): the mother is entitled until the girl menstruates.
This is the starting point before considering the fatwa.
2
Analyze the shift to the mufta bihi
Note that due to 'corruption of time,' the fatwa relies on later narrations: the mother's custody ends when the girl becomes 'desired' (shahwah), estimated at nine years by Abu al-Layth.
This is the operative position for fatwa.
3
Define shahwah
Understand that 'desire' (shahwah) is not fixed but varies by individual; it is the point at which a girl is fit for men and can be approached for marriage.
The grandmother's custody follows the same rule.
4
Examine the grandmother's rights and disputes
Review the cases from al-Zahiriyyah: the grandmother’s entitlement and how disputes between parents are resolved using the principle of farash (marriage bed).
This illustrates practical application.

أولى منها وذا محتمل، فإن أحضر الأب امرأة فقال هذه ابنتك وهذا ابني منها وقالت الجدة ما هذه ابنتي وقد ماتت ابنتي أم هذا الصبي فالقول في هذا قول الرجل والمرأة التي . ويدفع الصبي إليه لأن الفراش لهما فيكون الولد لهما، وصار هذا كالزوجين إذا كان بينهما ولد فقالت المرأة هو ابني من زوج آخر وقال الرجل هو ابني من امرأة أخرى فإنه يحكم بكونه ابناً لهما لأن الفراش لهما فيكون الولد لهما، وكذلك الجدة لو حضرت وقالت هذا ابن ابنتي من هذا الرجل وقد ماتت أمه فقال الرجل هذا ابني من غير ابنتك من امرأة لي فالقول قوله ويأخذ الصبي منها، ولو أحضر الرجل امرأة وقال هذا ابني من هذه لا من ابنتك وقالت الجدة ما هذه أمه بل أمه ابنتي وقالت التي أحضرها الرجل صدقت ما أنا بأمه وقد كذب هذا الرجل ولكني امرأته فإن الأب أولى به فيأخذه. وعلل الخصاف رحمه الله الكتاب فقال : لأنه لما قال هذا ابني من هذه المرأة فقد أنكر كونها جدة له فيكون منكراً الحق لها في الحضانة أصلاً وهي أقرت له بالحق ا هـ
And it is estimated at seven and with it until she menstruates, and others are more entitled to her until she desires, and there is no right for the enslaved woman and the umm walad due to frequency of corruption. In al-Khulasa and Ghayat al-Mufti: reliance is on these narrations due to the corruption of the time. So the conclusion is that the fatwa is contrary to the apparent narration, for it is stated in al-Tajnish that the apparent narration is that she (the mother) is more entitled to her until she menstruates, and they differed on the limit of desire. In al-Walwaljiyyah: there is no fixed limit for it because it varies by the woman's condition. In al-Tabyin and others: a girl of eleven years is desired in all their sayings. Abu al-Layth estimated it as nine years, and the fatwa is upon it. And the author indicated that if she is married before reaching puberty, her custody is not dropped. And it is said in al-Qunyah: the minor girl, if she is not desired and has a husband, the mother's right to custody drops as long as she is not fit for men, except in a narration from Abu Yusuf if she is taken pleasure from. Its apparent meaning is that if she is fit for men before puberty and her father has married her, then there is no custody for her mother by agreement, so the unrestricted statement of al-Mukhtasar needs qualification. Yes, according to the mufta bihi, it is apparent, and I have not seen the ruling on when the father and mother differ on her menstruation: the mother says she has not menstruated and the father says she has, or on puberty by age. It should be that the mother's statement is taken, just as if he claimed to have married her and she denied, since he claims the dropping of her right while she denies it. His statement: (And others are more entitled to her until she desires) meaning other than the mother and grandmother are more entitled to the minor girl until she desires, so the father takes her. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: until she is independent because she cannot manage her service, hence she is not hired for service, so the purpose is not achieved, unlike the mother and grandmother because they are capable of that legally. And he made it unrestricted regarding the grandmother, so it includes his grandmother from his mother and his father, as in Fath al-Qadir. In al-Zahiriyyah: if a woman came with a boy seeking maintenance from his father, saying 'This is the son of my daughter from you, and his mother has died, so give me his maintenance,' and the father said 'You have spoken the truth, this is my son from your daughter, but his mother is not dead, she is in my house,' and he wanted to take the boy from her, he cannot do so until the judge knows about the mother and she comes and takes him, because when he acknowledged that she is the grandmother of the boy, he acknowledged that she has the right of custody, then he claims the existence of one who is more entitled than her, and this is plausible. If the father brought a woman and said 'This is your daughter and this is my son from her,' and the grandmother said 'This is not my daughter, and my daughter, the mother of this boy, has died,' then the statement in this is the statement of the man and the woman. And the boy is given to him because the bed (marriage) belongs to them both, so the child is theirs. This is like the case of a married couple having a child, and the woman says 'He is my son from another husband' and the man says 'He is my son from another woman,' then it is judged that he is a son to both, because the bed is for both so the child is theirs. Similarly, if the grandmother came and said 'This is the son of my daughter from this man, and his mother has died,' and the man said 'This is my son from other than your daughter, from my wife,' then his statement is upheld and he takes the boy from her. And if the man brought a woman and said 'This is my son from this one, not from your daughter,' and the grandmother said 'This is not his mother; rather his mother is my daughter,' and the woman brought by the man said 'He has spoken the truth, I am not his mother, and this man has lied, but I am his wife,' then the father is more entitled and he takes him. Al-Khassaf explained the book, saying: because when he said 'This is my son from this woman,' he denied that she is a grandmother to him, so he becomes a denier of her right to custody originally, and she acknowledged his right.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the duration of custody. It begins by stating the established ruling: the mother's custody ends when the girl menstruates or reaches the age of menstruation (estimated at seven years). However, due to corruption of the times, the fatwa is that the mother retains custody until the girl actually menstruates, which aligns with the apparent narration despite contrary practice. The commentator then discusses the limit of 'desire' (shahwah) when the father becomes entitled: it is not fixed but varies; Abu al-Layth set it at nine years, and the fatwa is on that. He then addresses cases where the girl is married before puberty: if she is not yet desired, the mother's custody remains; if she is desired, the mother loses custody by agreement except in one narration from Abu Yusuf. A dispute between parents about menstruation or puberty is resolved by taking the mother's word. Next, the matn phrase 'وغيرهما أحق بها حتى تشتهي' is explained: others (father, etc.) are entitled to the girl until she desires men, because she cannot manage her own service. The grandmother is included from both sides. The passage concludes with a detailed case from al-Zahiriyyah about a grandmother claiming custody and the father disputing, illustrating the application of the rules on acknowledgment and the bed (farash).

Key Terms
حضانة (hadhanah): Legal custody and care of a child, primarily a right of the mother until certain age
شهوة (shahwah): The onset of sexual desire in a girl, which triggers the transfer of custody from mother to father
فراش (farash): The marriage bed; legal presumption that a child born to a married couple belongs to the husband

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school, when does the mother's custody of her daughter end?
A) When the daughter turns seven years old
B) When the daughter begins to menstruate
C) When the daughter becomes 'desired' (tushtaha)
D) When the daughter reaches puberty, usually at 15 years

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's operative ruling for custody duration uses the criterion of 'desire' (shahwah), not menstruation or a fixed age.

No Custody for Enslaved Women: Rationale and Detail
Commentary on the rule that enslaved women have no custody rights until freed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on custody of enslaved women, explain the rationale of occupation with service, and apply the rule to different categories (amah, umm walad, mudabbara, mukataba).

Core Idea
Slavery nullifies custody rights because the slave is occupied with the master's service; freedom restores them.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the foundational rule that an enslaved woman (amah) and an umm walad have no right to custody of their children while enslaved. The commentator expands on the rationale—their obligation to serve the master prevents them from fulfilling custody duties—and then details the ruling for various sub-categories. Understanding this reasoning helps you apply the rule consistently and recognize when custody rights revive upon emancipation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling and its scope
The passage begins by stating that both the amah (enslaved woman) and the umm walad (slave mother of master's child) have no right to custody until they are freed. Note that this applies to all enslaved women, including the mudabbara (promised freedom after master's death) and the mukataba (under contract of manumission) for children born before the contract.
The key condition is slavery; once freedom is attained, the right returns.
2
Understand the rationale (illa)
The reason given is that enslaved women are 'occupied with the service of the master' (عجزهما عن الحضانة بالاشتغال بخدمة المولى). This rational ('illa) is based on their legal incapacity to dedicate time and effort to child-rearing while bound to serve. This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) rather than a direct textual citation.
The 'illa explains why even an umm walad, despite her special status, still has no custody until free.
3
Distinguish between children born before and after kitaba
For a mukataba, if she gives birth after entering the kitaba contract, that child falls under her kitaba and she becomes more entitled to custody than others. If born before, she is treated like a regular enslaved woman with no right.
This nuance shows that the 'illa is not absolute; the child's inclusion in the kitaba changes the legal relation.
4
Apply the ruling based on child's status (free or slave)
The commentary adds detail: if the child is a slave, the master is entitled to custody, regardless of whether the father is free or slave. The mother, even if freed, has no right because the child remains a slave. If the child is free, custody belongs to the free relatives, not to the mother's master.
This clarifies that the mother's own freedom is necessary but not sufficient if the child is still enslaved.
5
Summarize the condition and its removal
The overarching principle: freedom of the custodian is a condition (shart) for the right of custody. Slavery is a preventer (mani'). Once the mother is emancipated, the condition is fulfilled and the right is established.
This is captured in the conditions_pillars block: shurut: freedom; mawani: slavery.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم تعتقا والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل الأديان ولا خيار للولد ذكراً كان أو أنثى ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثم . أمه قوله : (ولا حق للأمة وأم الولد ما لم تعتقا) لعجزهما عن الحضانة بالاشتغال بخدمة المولى، وإذا أعتقتا صارتا حرتين أو آن ثبوت الحق. ودخل تحت الأمة المدبرة لوجود الرق فيها، وكذا المكاتبة داخلة تحت الأمة بالنسبة إلى الولد المولود قبل الكتابة، وأما إذا ولدته بعد الكتابة فهي أولى بحضانته من غيرها لأنه صار داخلاً في كتابتها . وأراد بالحق المنفي حق الحضانة قالوا: ولا يفرق بينه وبين للنهي عن ذلك. ولم يذكر المصنف أن الحق في حضانة ولد الأمة للمولى أو لغيره والحق التفصيل ؛ فإن كان الصغير رقيقاً فمولاه أحق به حراً كان أبوه أو عبداً، وكذا لو عتقت أمه بعد وضعه فلا حق لها في حضانته إنما الحق للمولى، سواء كانت منكوحة أبيه أو فارقها لأنه مملوكه وأما إذا كان حراً فالحضانة لأقربائه الأحرار إن كانت أمة أمة لا لمولاها ولا لمولاه الذي ،أعتقه وإن أعتقت كانت الحضانة لها
Unless they are emancipated, and the dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he does not comprehend religions, and there is no choice for the child, male or female, and a divorced woman shall not travel with her child except to her homeland if he has married her. Then his mother. When they are emancipated, they become free and the right is established. The mudabbara is included under the enslaved woman because of the existence of slavery in her. Similarly, the mukataba is included under the enslaved woman concerning a child born before the kitaba. But if she gives birth after the kitaba, she is more entitled to his custody than others because he becomes included in her kitaba. By 'right' is meant the right of custody. They said: there is no distinction between it and... due to the prohibition of that. The correct is detailed: if the young child is a slave, then his master is more entitled whether his father is free or a slave. Likewise, if his mother is emancipated after his birth, she has no right to his custody; the right belongs to the master, whether she was married to his father or separated from him because he is his slave. Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'ولا حق للأمة وأم الولد ما لم تعتقا'. It explains that an enslaved woman (أمة) and an umm walad (a slave woman who has borne her master's child) have no right to custody of their children because they are occupied serving their master. Upon emancipation, they become free and acquire the right. The ruling extends to the mudabbara (a slave promised freedom upon the master's death) and the mukataba (a slave with a contract of manumission) for children born before the kitaba. However, a child born to a mukataba after the kitaba falls under her kitaba, so she has custody priority over others. The commentary clarifies that the negation of right refers specifically to custody. It then details the proper ruling: if the child is a slave, the master is entitled regardless of whether the father is free or slave; the mother even if freed has no right. If the child is free, custody belongs to free relatives, not to the mother's master or anyone else. The passage emphasizes that the mother's custody only revives upon her emancipation.

Key Terms
أمة (amah): An enslaved woman who has not borne her master's child
أم ولد (umm walad): An enslaved woman who has borne a child to her master
مكاتبة (mukatabah): An enslaved woman who has a contract of manumission (kitaba)
مدبرة (mudabbara): An enslaved woman promised freedom upon her master's death

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She has custody only if the father is free
C) She has no custody rights until she is freed
D) She has custody rights but they are suspended

Key Takeaway
Slavery is a complete legal preventer of custody; only emancipation restores the mother's right, regardless of her status as umm walad or otherwise.

Custody of a Dhimmi Mother Over Her Muslim Child
Commentary on the dhimmi mother's custody right until aql al-adyan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the dhimmi mother's custody and explain the conditions under which custody is removed.

Core Idea
A dhimmi mother retains custody until the child comprehends religions; removal is wajib when risk is clear.

Custody law balances the mother's natural compassion with the protection of the child's faith. The Hanafi school gives a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected) mother priority in custody of her Muslim child until the child can distinguish between religions. Once that threshold is reached, custody transfers to prevent potential religious harm. This lesson unpacks the ruling, its basis in istihsan, and important refinements from later Hanafi works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its basis
Locate the matn phrase: 'والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل الأديان'. The ruling is that the dhimmi mother has priority over the Muslim child until the child reaches the age of comprehending religions. The basis is her superior compassion (shafaqah).
This is a default rule; the exception is when harm is likely.
2
Determine the threshold for removal
The phrase 'ما لم يعقل الأديان' sets a cognitive threshold: the child must be able to understand the difference between religions. Typically this occurs around age 7-9. At that point, custody is removed to the father or a Muslim relative.
Al-Hidayah adds a second threshold: fear of assimilation (even before comprehension).
3
Understand the scope of 'dhimmi' and exclusions
The term 'dhimmi' includes Kitabiyya (Jews/Christians) and Majusiyya (Zoroastrians). It excludes an apostate mother (murtaddah) because she is imprisoned and cannot care for the child. If she repents, she regains custody.
This is from the commentary's expansion on the matn.
4
Analyze the exception for feeding haram
Even if the mother feeds the child pork or wine, she is not removed; rather, a Muslim supervisor is appointed to oversee the child. This shows that not every religious risk triggers removal—only those affecting the child's core beliefs.
This is from Sharh al-Niqayah cited in the commentary.
5
Recognize the extension to female relatives
The ruling extends to all dhimmi female custodians: the grandmother (um al-umm) and other female kin are treated like the mother. This is from Khizanat al-Akmal.
This ensures consistency across custody cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل الأديان لأن الحضانة تبتني على الشفقة وهي أشفق عليه فيكون الدفع إليها أنظر له، فإذا عقل الأديان ينزع منها لاحتمال الضرر. وأطلق الذمية فشمل الكتابية والمجوسية كما في غاية البيان وغيره وقيد بها للاحتراز عن المرتدة لأنه لا حق لها فيها لأنها تحبس وتضرب فلا تتفرغ له، ولا في دفعه إليها نظر إذا أسلمت وتابت يسلم الولد إليها. وقد جمع في الهداية بين شيئين فقال ما لم يعقل الأديان أو يخاف أن يألف الكفر. فظاهره أنه إذا خيف أن يألف الكفر نزع منها وإن لم يعقل ديناً وهي ورادة على المصنف المقتصر على الأول. وفي شرح النقاية : لو خيف أن تغذيه بلحم خنزير أو خمر لم ينزع منها بل يضم إلى ناس من المسلمين والتقييد بالأم اتفاقي إذ كل حاضنة ذمية كذلك كما صرح في خزانة الأكمل : وأم الأم بمنزلة الأم مسلمة كانت أو كتابية أو مجوسية، وكذا كل من نساء القرابة فهي بمنزلة الأم ا هـ. قوله : (لم ينزع منها بل يضم إلى ناس من المسلمين ليس في الفتح والنهر قوله «لم ينزع منها» وأيضاً فظاهر أنه يضم إلى ناس من المسلمين أن ينزع منها إلا أن يكون المعنى يضم إليهم عندها تأمل .
His statement: (And the dhimmi woman is more entitled to her Muslim child as long as he does not comprehend the religions because custody is based on compassion, and she is more compassionate towards him, so entrusting to her is more beneficial. When he comprehends the religions, he is taken from her due to the possibility of harm. He made 'dhimmi' unrestricted, so it includes the Kitabiyya and the Majusiyya, as in Ghayat al-Bayan and others; and he restricted it to exclude the apostate because she has no right in it since she is imprisoned and beaten and cannot devote herself to him. And there is no consideration in entrusting him to her if she returns to Islam and repents, the child is given to her. In al-Hidayah, he combined two matters, saying: as long as he does not comprehend the religions or it is feared that he will become accustomed to disbelief. So apparently, if it is feared that he will become accustomed to disbelief, he is taken from her even if he does not comprehend a religion, and this is an objection to the author who restricted to the first. In Sharh al-Niqayah: if it is feared that she will feed him pork or wine, he is not taken from her; rather, he is placed with Muslims. And the restriction to the mother is incidental, for every dhimmi custodial woman is like that, as stated in Khizanat al-Akmal: the mother's mother is like the mother, whether Muslim, Kitabiyya, or Majusiyya. Similarly, every female relative is like the mother. His statement: (he is not taken from her but is placed with Muslims) is not in al-Fath and al-Nahr; it says 'he is not taken from her'; also apparently that placing him with Muslims means removing him from her unless the meaning is placing him with them in her presence. Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn phrase 'والذمية أحق بولدها المسلم ما لم يعقل الأديان'. The ruling is that a dhimmi (non-Muslim protected) mother has priority in custody of her Muslim child as long as the child has not reached an age where he understands religious differences. The basis is her natural compassion, which outweighs the risk until the child can comprehend. Once he does, custody transfers to the father or Muslim relative to prevent potential harm. The term 'dhimmi' includes both Kitabiyya (Jews/Christians) and Majusiyya (Zoroastrians), but excludes an apostate (murtaddah) because she loses all rights and is imprisoned. If the apostate mother repents, she regains custody. The commentary notes that al-Hidayah adds a second condition: even if the child does not yet comprehend, if there is fear he will become accustomed to disbelief, custody is removed. This is a refinement. A crucial point is that even if the dhimmi mother might feed the child impermissible food (pork, wine), she is not removed entirely; rather, a Muslim supervisor is appointed to oversee without disrupting custody. The principle extends to all female relatives: the grandmother (um al-umm) and other female kin are treated like the mother in this rule. The passage ends with a textual critical note clarifying a phrase in the commentary.

Key Terms
ذمية (dhimmiyyah): A non-Muslim female citizen of the Islamic state (Jew, Christian, Zoroastrian) under contract of protection
عقل الأديان (aql al-adyan): The ability to comprehend and distinguish between different religions, typically around age 7-9

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only if she feeds the child pork or wine
D) When the child reaches puberty

Key Takeaway
The dhimmi mother's custody is protected by compassion, but removed when the child can discern religions or when assimilation is feared.

No Choice for the Child in Hanafi Custody Law
Hanafi position against child custody choice, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a child has no choice in custody, explain the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i hadith argument, and identify the practical difference between the two schools.

Core Idea
Hanafi law gives the child no choice in custody; fixed rules based on age and gender govern the transfer.

Custody disputes often raise the question: should the child's preference matter? This lesson examines the Hanafi position that a child lacks the legal capacity to choose between parents. We will explore the arguments, the Shafi'i counter-argument from a hadith, and how the Hanafi school resolves the issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi Ruling
Locate the statement 'لا خيار للولد عندنا' in the text. This is the core ruling: according to the Hanafi school, a child has no choice at all, whether male or female.
This ruling applies regardless of the child's age or gender; no khiyar.
2
Understand the Shafi'i Objection
Note that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the child does have a choice, based on a hadith where the Prophet gave a child the option between parents. Identify the opposing school and its proof.
The hadith is the key point of contention.
3
Follow the Hanafi Rebuttal
Examine the three Hanafi responses: (1) the child's deficient intellect causes him to choose immediate pleasure over long-term benefit; (2) the Companions did not practice giving choice; (3) the hadith is interpreted either as a specific case where the Prophet prayed for divine guidance for the child, or it applies only to a mature (baligh) child.
The Hanafi argument prioritizes the child's welfare over expressed preference.
4
Identify the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
Work out the practical outcome: Hanafi school never allows the child's preference to overrule the fixed custody rules; Shafi'i school gives the child who has reached the age of discernment (typically around 7 years) the right to choose between parents, and the custodian may be determined accordingly.
This difference directly affects how a family judge would rule in a custody case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ولا خيار للولد عندنا ذكراً كان أو أنثى) وقال الشافعي : لهما الخيار لأن خير . ولنا أنه لقصور . عقله يختار من عنده الدعة والراحة لتخليته بينه وبين اللعب فلا يتحقق النظر، وقد صح أن الصحابة رضي الله عنهم لم يخيروا . وأما الحديث قلنا قد قال عليه الصلاة والسلام اللهم اهده فوفق لاختيار الأنظر بدعائه عليه السلام، أو يحمل على ما إذا كان بالغاً. والمراد بعدم تخييره عندنا أنه إذا بلغ السن الذي ينزع من الأم يأخذه الأب ولا
His statement: (And there is no choice for the child according to us, male or female). Al-Shafi'i said: they have the choice because he chose. Our argument: because his intellect is deficient, he would choose the one with ease and leisure so that he can play, so the objective (best interest) is not achieved. And it is established that the Companions did not give the child choice. As for the hadith, we say: the Prophet ﷺ said 'O Allah, guide him' and he was guided to choose the more beneficial by his prayer, or it is interpreted as applying to a mature child. The meaning of having no choice according to us is that when he reaches the age at which he is taken from the mother, the father takes him, and
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-school dispute (with the Shafi'i school) on whether a child has the right to choose which parent to live with. The Hanafi position is that the child has no choice; custody is determined by fixed rules (mother until an age, then father). Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the child should be given a choice because of a hadith. The Hanafi response: the child's intellect is immature and he will choose for immediate pleasure, not long-term benefit. The hadith is interpreted as either a specific case where the Prophet prayed for the child's guidance, or it applies only to a child who has reached puberty. The practical outcome: in the Hanafi school, when the child reaches the age for custody transfer (e.g., a girl after shahwah, a boy after seven), the father automatically takes over; no choice is given. The text ends abruptly due to page break.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): The child's right to choose between mother and father for custody

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the child not get a choice in custody?
A) Because the child always prefers the mother
B) Because the child's intellect is deficient and he would choose for immediate pleasure
C) Because the father is always the better guardian
D) Because the hadith on choice is abrogated

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law gives the child no choice in custody; fixed rules based on age and gender determine the parent, and the Shafi'i hadith is interpreted restrictively.

Child's Choice Upon Reaching Age of Discernment
Ruling on child's choice and the case of a lunatic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a child's choice between parents upon reaching the age of discernment, and distinguish the case of a lunatic.

Core Idea
A discerning child chooses between parents; a lunatic has no choice and stays with the mother until puberty, then the father.

In Hanafi custody law, the child's own preference matters once he or she reaches the age of discernment (around 7 years). However, this right is not absolute—the child must be sane and capable of making a reasoned decision. This lesson examines the ruling from the Hanafi school, including the special case of the lunatic child (ma'tuh), clarifying when the mother's custody ends and the father assumes responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of children
The passage distinguishes between a discerning child (sane) and a lunatic child (ma'tuh). The ruling differs based on mental capacity.
2
State the ruling for a discerning child
The child is given a choice between parents. This is a recognized right in the Hanafi school.
The choice is typically granted to children who have reached the age of tamyiz (around 7-9 years).
3
State the ruling for a lunatic child
The lunatic is not given a choice; he remains with the mother until the age of puberty (the 'mentioned age'), after which he goes to the father.
The text clarifies that this is the Hanafi position (عندنا).
4
Note the gap in the author's text
The author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq did not specify whether a child who reaches puberty independently lives alone or stays with the father. This gap is addressed in later works.
The commentator points out this omission.
5
Apply the ruling
Determine custody based on the child's capacity and age. For a sane child, ask his preference; for a lunatic, apply the fixed rule.

Custody Conditions for Mature Children in Hanafi Fiqh
Separate rulings for mature daughter and son

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a father may take a mature daughter or son into his custody according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Father's custody over mature child is conditional on the child's trustworthiness and maturity.

This passage from al-Zahiriyyah details how a father's right to custody changes once a child becomes mature. You will learn that a virgin daughter may be kept if young, but once mature she may live independently; a previously married daughter may only be kept if she is not trustworthy; and a mature son cannot be forced unless he is untrustworthy. Only the father and grandfather retain the right to reinstate custody if needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main actors
Note the two categories of daughters (bikr and thayyib) and sons. For each, the ruling depends on the child's maturity and trustworthiness.
2
Analyze the father's right for each category
For a virgin daughter: father may take her even if no fear, provided she is young. Once she is mature and intelligent, she may choose residence. For a thayyib: father may only take her if she is not trustworthy over herself. For a son: father may not take him if he is independent and trustworthy; if not trustworthy, father may take him but is not obliged to support him.
3
Distinguish father/grandfather from other relatives
Only the father and grandfather have the original right to take custody; other relatives like brothers or uncles cannot take her even if she is untrustworthy, because they never had that right initially.
4
Understand the rationale (al-farq)
The father and grandfather had the right to take custody initially, so if the child becomes untrustworthy, they may reinstate it. Others never had that initial right, so they cannot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الظهيرية : فإذا بلغت الجارية مبلغ النساء فإن كانت بكراً كان للأب أن يضمها إلى نفسه، وإن كانت ثيباً فليس له ذلك إلا إذا لم تكن مأمونة على نفسها . والغلام إذا عقل واجتمع رأيه واستغنى عن الأب ليس للأب أن يضمه إلى نفسه إلا إذا لم يكن مأموناً على نفسه كان له أن يضمه إلى نفسه، وليس عليه نفقته إلا أن يتبرع. ومتى كانت الجارية بكراً يضمها إلى نفسه وإن كان لا يخاف عليها الفساد إذا كانت حديثة السن، أما إذا دخلت في السن واجتمع لها رأي وعقلت فليس للأولياء حق الضم ولها أن تنزل حيث أحبت حيث لا يتخوف عليها، وإن كانت ثيباً مخوفاً عليها وليس لها أب ولا جد ولكن لها أخ أو عم ليس له ولاية الضم إلى نفسه بخلاف الأب والجد. والفرق أن الأب والجد كان لهما ولاية الضم في الابتداء فجاز أن يعيداها إلى حجرهما إذا لم تكن مأمونة، أما غير الأب والجد فلم يكن له ولاية الضم في الابتداء فلا يكون له ولاية الإعادة أيضاً.
And in al-Zahiriyyah: When the girl reaches the age of women, if she is a virgin, the father may take her into his custody; if she is previously married (thayyib), he may not unless she is not trustworthy over herself. And the boy, when he becomes intelligent, sound of opinion, and independent of the father, the father may not take him into his custody unless he is not trustworthy over himself; then he may take him, and he is not obligated for his maintenance unless he voluntarily provides. Whenever the girl is a virgin, he may take her even if he does not fear corruption if she is young; once she is older and sound of opinion and intelligent, then the guardians have no right to take her, and she may live wherever she wishes as long as no harm is feared. If she is thayyib and feared for her corruption, and she has no father or grandfather but has a brother or uncle, he does not have the right to take her into his custody, unlike the father and grandfather. The difference is that the father and grandfather had the right of taking her into custody initially, so they may return her to their care if she is not trustworthy. Others besides the father and grandfather never had that right initially, so they do not have the right to reinstate it either.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage details the custody rights over a mature daughter or son according to the Hanafi school. A mature virgin daughter may be kept by her father even if no moral danger is feared, but once she is older and mature, she may live independently. For a thayyib (previously married), the father may only keep her if she is not trustworthy. For a boy who is mature and independent, the father cannot force him to stay unless the boy is untrustworthy. The key distinction is between father/grandfather and other relatives: only the father and grandfather have the original right to take the child into custody; others like uncles or brothers do not have that right. This is a detailed ruling on post-puberty custody rights.

Key Terms
البكر (al-bikr): A virgin female who has never been married.
الثيب (al-thayyib): A female who has been previously married.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Always, because he is the father
B) Only if she is not trustworthy over herself
C) Only if she is a virgin
D) Never, after she reaches puberty

Key Takeaway
A father's custody over a mature child is conditional: the child may live independently if trustworthy and mature; the father's right revives if the child is not trustworthy.

Custody Rights: Father/Grandfather vs. Other Relatives
Ruling on relatives without custody right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why only the father and grandfather have the right to take a thayyib woman into protective custody, and identify the legal basis for excluding brothers and uncles.

Core Idea
Only father and grandfather have the inherent right to take a woman into protective custody; other relatives lack this authority.

In Hanafi custody law, the father and grandfather hold a unique position. This lesson examines why a brother or uncle cannot force a mature woman to live with them, even if she is not trustworthy. The distinction hinges on whether the relative ever had an initial right to custody.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The ruling applies to a thayyib (non-virgin) woman whose behavior is feared (i.e., potentially untrustworthy).
This is not about a virgin; the ruling may differ.
2
State the ruling for relatives
Brother or uncle has no right to take her into his custody, even if he is trustworthy.
This is despite being an 'asabah guardian.
3
Contrast with father/grandfather
Father and grandfather do have that right; they can reinstate custody if she becomes untrustworthy.
The reason given: they originally had the right of custody.
4
Analyze the rationale
The difference is that father and grandfather had initial custody (when she was a minor), so they can return her to their care. Others never had that initial right.
This is a key Hanafi principle: rights derived from prior authority.
5
Recognize the judge's role as an alternative
If no suitable guardian exists, the judge steps in (covered in next lesson).
This lesson focuses only on relatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت ثيباً مخوفاً عليها وليس لها أب ولا جد ولكن لها أخ أو عم ليس له ولاية الضم إلى نفسه بخلاف الأب والجد. والفرق أن الأب والجد كان لهما ولاية الضم في الابتداء فجاز أن يعيداها إلى حجرهما إذا لم تكن مأمونة، أما غير الأب والجد فلم يكن له ولاية الضم في الابتداء فلا يكون له ولاية الإعادة أيضاً. وإن لم يكن لها أب ولا جد ولا عصبة أو كان لها عصبة مفسد فللقاضي أن ينظر في حالها، فإن كانت مأمونة خلاها تنفرد بالسكني، سواء كانت بكراً أو ثيباً وإلا وضعها عند امرأة أمينة ثقة تقدر على الحفظ لأنه جعل ناظراً للمسلمين؛ كذا في التبيين.
If she is a thayyib feared for her corruption and she has no father or grandfather but has a brother or uncle, he does not have the right to take her into his custody, unlike the father and grandfather. The difference is that the father and grandfather had the right of taking her into custody initially, so they may return her to their care if she is not trustworthy. Others besides the father and grandfather never had that right initially, so they do not have the right to reinstate it either. And if she has no father, grandfather, or guardian (asabah), or has a corrupt guardian, then the judge should examine her situation. If she is trustworthy, he lets her live alone, whether she is virgin or thayyib. Otherwise, he places her with a trustworthy woman capable of guarding her, because he is appointed as supervisor for the Muslims; as in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage clarifies that brothers, uncles, and other relatives do not have the same custody rights as the father and grandfather. They cannot force a mature woman to live with them, even if she is not trustworthy. Only the judge can intervene. The judge's role: if the woman is trustworthy, she can live independently; if not, he appoints a trustworthy woman to guard her. This is a very important ruling in Hanafi law emphasizing the father's and grandfather's unique position and the judge's authority as guardian for Muslims.

Key Terms
العصبة (al-'asabah): Male agnate relatives (like brother, uncle) who have guardianship in marriage and inheritance but not necessarily custody.
القاضي (al-qadi): The judge who acts as a supervisor for Muslims (nazir li-l-muslimin) to protect those without suitable guardians.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They are not mahram to the woman.
B) They never had the initial right of custody.
C) The woman's consent is required and she does not consent.
D) They are not the judge.

Key Takeaway
Only the father and grandfather have the inherent right to take a thayyib woman into protective custody; brothers and uncles lack this authority.

The Judge's Role in Custody When No Suitable Guardian
Ruling on judge's intervention and options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the judge's procedure when a woman has no father, grandfather, or has a corrupt guardian, and distinguish between the two outcomes based on her trustworthiness.

Core Idea
When no suitable guardian exists, the judge acts as supervisor for Muslims, allowing a trustworthy woman to live alone or placing an untrustworthy one with a trustworthy woman.

After confirming that no father, grandfather, or trustworthy 'asabah exists, the judge must assess the woman's situation. This lesson details the judge's authority and the two possible rulings: leaving her alone if she is trustworthy, or appointing a trustworthy female guardian if she is not.

Guided Path

This scenario follows the first part of the passage.

The judge examines whether she is 'ma'muna' (trustworthy, i.e., not likely to fall into sin or harm).
This is a factual determination.
3
Apply ruling if trustworthy
If she is trustworthy, the judge leaves her to live alone (تنفرد بالسكنى), regardless of whether she is virgin or thayyib.
She enjoys full independence.
4
Apply ruling if not trustworthy
If she is not trustworthy, the judge places her with a trustworthy woman capable of guarding her.
The guardian is a woman, not a man.
5

The judge is appointed as 'nazir li-l-muslimin' (supervisor for Muslims), giving him broad authority to protect those without proper guardians.

Divorced Mother's Travel with Child: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on travel destination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a divorced mother's travel with her child to a city where her husband married her but which is not her hometown, and state the preferred view with its evidence.

Core Idea
A divorced mother may only travel to her original hometown; traveling to the husband's new city is disputed and prohibited by the preferred view.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a divorced mother wishes to travel with her child to a city where her husband married her but which is not her own hometown. The Hanafi school has two narrations on this matter, and al-Hidayah rules against such travel. We will explore the proofs and the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
State the default rule
Identify the general ruling: a divorced mother should not travel with her child to a place that harms the father. She may only travel to her hometown where the husband married her.
This is the agreed-upon foundation.
2
Identify the disputed scenario
The dispute arises when the mother wants to go to a city different from her hometown but where the husband married her. Does the marriage contract grant her the right to move there?
The source calls this a city 'other than her hometown'.
3
Present the two Hanafi views
One view (Book of Talaq) says she may not. Another view (al-Jami' al-Saghir) says she may, by analogy to sale.
Both are recorded in al-Hidayah.
4
Analyze the proofs
The view against is based on custom: marriage in a foreign land does not imply staying there. The view for uses qiyas: the contract imposes its rulings where it is concluded, like a sale requires delivery.
A hadith 'whoever settles in a town is from them' is cited but interpreted differently.
5
Determine the preferred view and its consequence
Al-Hidayah prefers the first view (prohibition). Practical consequence: if the mother takes the child to such a city, the father may demand return, and judges may not enforce the mother's move.
This is the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا تسافر مطلقة بولدها إلا إلى وطنها وقد نكحها ثم لأن في السفر به اضراراً بأبيه، فإذا خرجت به إلى وطنها وقد كان تزوجها الزوج فيه فلها ذلك لأنه التزم المقام فيه عرفاً وشرعاً. قال عليه السلام من تأهل ببلدة فهو منهم (۱) ولهذا يصير الحربي به ذمياً؛ في الهداية. ودفعة في الكافي بأن المصرح به أن الحربي لا يصير بتأهله في دار الإسلام ذمياً لإمكان أن يطلقها ثم يعود إلى دار الحرب، وإنما ذلك في الحربية إذا تزوجت فإنها تصير ذمية، وما في التبيين من إبدال الحربي بالحربية لا يناسب المقام لأن الكلام في الرجل وشرط المصنف لجواز سفرها به أمرين واتفقوا أنه ليس لها السفر به إلى مصر لم يتزوجها فيه، واختلفوا فيما إذا أرادت الخروج إلى مصر غيره وطنها وقد كان التزوج فيه. أشار في الكتاب إلى أنه ليس لها ذلك وهذا رواية كتاب الطلاق، وذكر في الجامع الصغير أن لها ذلك لأن العقد متى وجد في مكان يوجب أحكامه فيه كما يوجب البيع التسليم في ومن ذلك حق إمساك الأولاد وجه الأول أن التزوج في دار الغربة ليس التزاماً للمكث فيه عرفاً وهذا أصح؛ كذا في الهداية.
His saying: 'And a divorced woman should not travel with her child except to her hometown where her husband had married her because traveling with him harms his father. If she goes out with him to her hometown where the husband had married her, she may do so because he undertook to live there customarily and legally. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Whoever settles in a town, he is from them' and thus a harbi becomes a dhimmi by it; as in al-Hidayah. And it is refuted in al-Kafi by stating that the explicit ruling is that a harbi does not become a dhimmi by settling in Dar al-Islam because it is possible he may divorce her and return to dar al-harb. That only applies to a harbiyya woman if she marries, then she becomes a dhimmi; and what is in al-Tabyin of substituting al-harbi with al-harbiyya is not appropriate here because the discussion is about the man. And the author stipulated for the permissibility of her traveling with him two matters, and they agreed that she may not travel with him to a city where he did not marry her. They differed about when she wants to go out to a city other than her hometown where he had married her. The book indicated that she may not do so, and this is the narration of the Book of Talaq. It is mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir that she may do so because the contract, when concluded in a place, necessitates its rulings there, just as a sale necessitates delivery; and from that is the right to retain children. The first view is that marriage in a foreign land is not an undertaking to stay there customarily, and this is more correct; as in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage addresses the conditions under which a divorced mother may travel with her child. The general rule is that she cannot travel if it harms the father. However, she may travel to her hometown where her husband married her because he implicitly agreed to reside there. The text includes a hadith about settling in a town. There is a dispute (ikhtilaf) between the narrations: One view (Book of Talaq) says she cannot go to any other town, even if the marriage was conducted there, while another view (al-Jami' al-Saghir) says she can, because the marriage contract in that place gives rights similar to a sale. The preferred view is that marriage in a foreign land does not imply permanent residence, so she cannot force the child to move there. This is a classic intra-Hanafi dispute recorded in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
المطلقة (al-mutallaqah): A divorced woman, specifically a bain (irrevocable) divorce after her 'iddah has ended.
الوطن (al-watan): The hometown where the woman originally belonged, and where the marriage was contracted.
التأهل (al-ta'ahhul): Settling in a town, taking up residence with the intention to stay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in al-Hidayah, may a divorced mother travel with her child to a city where her husband married her but which is not her original hometown?
A) Yes, because marriage in that city gives rights
B) No, because marriage in a foreign land does not imply staying there
C) Yes, always
D) Only if the father agrees

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view prohibits a divorced mother from taking her child to a city where she married but which is not her hometown, because marriage in a foreign land does not imply permanent residence.

Correcting the Author's Expression on the Mother's Travel with Her Child
Critical refinement of the author's terminology regarding mother's travel with child.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct fiqh expression for the mother's restriction in moving with her child and distinguish between shar'i and linguistic travel in this context.

Core Idea
The mother's right to move with her child depends on distance; the term 'safar' is imprecise.

The commentator in al-Bahr al-Raiq critiques the author's use of 'tusafir' (travel) in Kanz al-Daqaiq. He argues that whether travel is defined by sharia or language, it does not accurately capture the ruling. The correct expression specifies a significant distance between towns. This lesson clarifies the precise wording and its implications for custody rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested expression
The author used 'tusafir' (travel). Why is this problematic? The commentator argues that neither the shar'i definition (three days' journey) nor the linguistic definition (any journey) correctly applies.
The prohibition does not require a three-day distance, nor is it triggered by very short moves.
2
Understand the two definitions of 'safar'
Shari'i safar is a journey of three days and nights (approx. 48 miles). Linguistic safar is any journey. Both fail here: the first is too long, the second too short.
The commentator uses this to show the author's term is imprecise.
3
Recognize the correct expression
The correct phrase is 'ليس لها الخروج بالولد من بلدة إلى أخرى بينهما تفاوت' (she may not go out with the child from one town to another where there is a considerable distance). This captures the condition of distance precisely.
The key term is ‘تفاوت’ (tafawut) – significant difference.
4
Note the exceptions
Exception 1: Moving from a village to a city is allowed because it benefits the child's upbringing and causes no harm to the father. Exception 2: Moving to dar al-harb is prohibited if it harms the child or the Muslim/dhimmi parent.
Also, the ruling applies only to the divorced mother (bain) after 'iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي شرح النقاية : وإنما قال المصنف تسافر دون تخرج لأنه لو بين الموضعين تقارب بحيث يتمكن الأب من مطالعة ولده الرجوع إليه في نهاره جاز لها أن تنتقل إليه، سواء كان وطناً لها أو لم يكن وقع العقد فيه أو لم يقع، لأن الانتقال إلى قريب بمنزلة الانتقال من محلة إلى محلة في بلدة واحدة .ا.هـ. والذي يظهر عدم صحة التعبير بالسفر أو بالخروج على الإطلاق لأن السفر إن كان المراد به الشرعي لم يصح إذ لا يشترط في منعها عن الخروج به أن يكون بين الوطنيين ثلاثة أيام وإن كان المراد به السفر اللغوي لم أيضاً لأنه إذا كان بين المكانين تقارب لا تمنع مطلقاً فهو كالانتقال محلة إلى أخرى، من وكذا التعبير بمطلق الخروج لا يصح والعبارة الصحيحة ليس لها الخروج وبالولد من بلدة إلى أخرى بينهما تفاوت كما ذكرناه إلا إذا انتقلت من القرية إلى المصر فإن لها ذلك لأن فيه نظراً للصغير حيث يتخلق بأخلاق أهل المصر، وليس فيه ضرر بالأب وهي واردة على المصنف وفي عكسه ضرر بالصغير لتخلقه بأخلاق أهل السواد فليس لها ذلك مطلقاً. ويستثنى من جواز نقله إذا وجد الأمران في دار الحرب فليس لها أن تنقله إليها إذا كان وطنها ونكحها فيه لما فيه من الإضرار بالولد والوالد المسلم أو الذمي حتى لو كان الوالد والوالدة حربيين لها ذلك. وقيد بالمطلقة لأن المنكوحة ليس لها الخروج به من بلد إلى آخر مطلقاً لأن حق السكني للزوج بعد إيفاء المعجل خصوصاً بعدما خرجت معه وأراد بالمطلقة المبانة بعد انقضاء عدتها لأن المطلقة رجعياً حكمها حكم المنكوحة، ومعتدة البائن ليس لها الخروج قبل انقضاء العدة مطلقاً. وقيد بالأم لأن الأم لو ماتت وصارت الحضانة للجدة فليس لها أن تنتقل إلى مصرها
And in Sharh al-Niqayah: The author said 'tusafir' (travel) not 'takhruj' (go out) because if the two places are close enough that the father can see his child and return in the same day, she may move to him, whether it is her hometown or not, whether the contract took place there or not, because moving to a nearby place is like moving from one district to another in the same city. And it appears that the expression 'safar' or 'khuruj' is absolutely incorrect because if by 'safar' is meant the shar'i travel, it is not correct since the prohibition does not require a three-day distance; if by 'safar' is meant linguistic travel, it is also not correct because if the two places are close, she is not prohibited at all, it is like moving from one district to another. Similarly the expression 'mutlaq al-khuruj' is not correct. The correct expression is: 'She may not go out with the child from one town to another where there is a difference (distance) as we mentioned, except if she moves from a village to a city, because that is in the interest of the child where he adopts the manners of the city people, and there is no harm to the father. And this is an objection to the author. In the opposite case, there is harm to the child because of adopting rural manners, so she may not do so at all. An exception is made from the permissibility of moving when both conditions exist in dar al-harb: she may not move him there if it is her home and she was married there, because of harm to the child and the Muslim or dhimmi parent. Even if both parents are harbis, she may do so. It is restricted to the divorced woman because a wife cannot go out with him from one town to another at all, because the husband's right to housing is in effect after the prompt dower, especially after she has left with him. By 'mutallaqah' is meant the bain divorce after the 'iddah, because the raj'i divorce has the same ruling as the wife, and the woman in 'iddah of a bain divorce cannot go out before the 'iddah ends absolutely. It is restricted to the mother because if the mother dies and custody goes to the grandmother, she may not move to her city.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hāshiya) of the author's expression in Kanz al-Daqā'iq. The commentator argues that the author's use of 'tusāfir' (travel) is imprecise. If 'safar' means the shar'i travel (three days distance), then it is not correct because the prohibition does not require such a distance. If it means linguistic travel (any journey), it is also not correct because if the two places are close, she may move freely. The correct expression is 'al-khurūj bi al-walad min balda ilā ukhrā baynahumā tafāwut' (going out from one town to another between which there is a considerable distance). Exceptions: moving from village to city is allowed due to benefit for the child. Moving to dar al-harb is prohibited if it harms the child or the Muslim/dhimmi parent. The ruling applies only to the mother after a bain divorce and after 'iddah. If the mother dies, the grandmother does not have the same right. This shows the meticulousness of Hanafi jurists in terminology.

Key Terms
السفر الشرعي (al-safar al-shar'i): A journey of three days and three nights (or approximately 48 miles in Hanafi law) that changes certain rulings.
السفر اللغوي (al-safar al-lughawi): Any journey in the linguistic sense, regardless of distance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is the correct expression for the mother's restriction in moving with the child?
A) She may not travel with the child
B) She may not go out with the child from one town to another where there is considerable distance
C) She may not move even to a nearby house
D) She may not move except to her parents' home

Key Takeaway
The correct legal expression must precisely capture the condition of distance; imprecise terms can lead to incorrect rulings.

Mother's Right to Travel with Child: Conditions and Exceptions
Presents the ruling on mother's travel with child and clarifies legal terminology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a divorced mother may move her child to another city and apply the Hanafi preferred view.

Core Idea
The mother's right to move the child is restricted to significant distances, with exceptions for the child's welfare.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical custody question: after divorce, may the mother move the child to another city? The author presents the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explores an intra-Hanafi disagreement, and critiques the terminology used. By the end, you'll understand the precise conditions, exceptions, and the correct expression for this legal action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The source states two conditions for the mother to travel with the child: (1) the marriage must have occurred in the destination city, and (2) the mother must be a divorced woman (ba'in after 'iddah). Students should locate these in the text.
The author mentions 'شرط المصنف لجواز سفرها به أمرين'.
2
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a disagreement between al-Asl (the mother may not travel) and al-Jami' al-Saghir (she may). The preferred view (asahh) is that she may not, because marriage in a foreign land does not imply a permanent residence. Note the reasoning and the author's tarjih.
Refer to the phrase 'وهذا أصح' in the source.
3
Distinguish safar from khuruj
The author criticizes the use of 'safar' (travel) and clarifies that the correct term is 'khuruj' (moving). Understand why: legal travel (safar shar'i) is a specific distance, while the issue here is about moving the child to a distant place, regardless of the three-day journey.
The passage states 'الذي يظهر عدم صحة التعبير بالسفر'.
4
Apply the exceptions
Exceptions include: moving from a village to a city (for the child's benefit) and moving to a nearby place where the father can return to the child within a day. Also, moving to dar al-harb is prohibited if the father is Muslim/dhimmi. These exceptions modify the general prohibition.
Note the distinction between moving to a city vs. moving to a nearby place.
5
Summarize the final ruling
Compile the conditions and exceptions into a clear statement: a divorced mother (ba'in after 'iddah) may not move the child to a distant city unless it is to a more beneficial location (e.g., village to city) or a close distance. The grandmother does not have this right. The correct term is 'khuruj' (moving), not 'safar'.
The final ruling is conditioned on the child's welfare and the father's ability to supervise.

The book of divorce, chapter of custody. It was in its place. A sentence about a dhimmi woman, and what is in al-Tabyeen of replacing a harbi man with a harbi woman is not appropriate for the context because the discussion is about the man. The author (of Kanz) stipulated for the permissibility of her traveling with him two matters, and they agreed that she does not have the right to travel with him to a city in which he did not marry her. They differed regarding if she wants to go to a city other than her homeland while the marriage took place there. The Book (al-Asl) indicated that she does not have that, and this is the narration of the Book of Talaq. Al-Jami' al-Saghir mentioned that she has that because whenever a contract is found in a place, it necessitates its rulings therein, just as a sale necessitates delivery there, and from that is the right of keeping children. The first view (that marriage in a foreign land is not an undertaking to stay there customarily) is more correct; as in al-Hidayah. In Sharh al-Niqayah: The author (of Kanz) only said 'tusafiru' (she travels) without 'takhruju' (she goes out) because if there is closeness between the two places such that the father can supervise his child and return to him within the day, she may transfer to it, whether it is her homeland or not, whether the contract took place there or not, because moving to a nearby place is like moving from one neighborhood to another in a single city. End quote. What appears is the incorrectness of the expression 'safar' (travel) or 'khuruj' (exit) absolutely. Because if by 'safar' is meant the legal travel, it is incorrect since it is not a condition in preventing her from going out with him that there be three days between the two homelands. And if by 'safar' is meant the linguistic travel, it is also [?] because if there is closeness between the two places, she is not prevented absolutely, so it is like moving from one neighborhood to another. Likewise the expression of absolute exit is not correct. The correct expression is: she does not have the right to go out with the child from one town to another that has a difference between them as we mentioned, except if she moves from the village to the city, then she has that because in it is consideration for the child as he acquires the morals of city people, and there is no harm to the father. This is an objection on the author (of Kanz). In its opposite, there is harm to the child due to acquiring the morals of country folk, so she does not have that absolutely. An exception is made from the permissibility of moving him if the two matters exist in dar al-harb (hostile territory); she does not have the right to move him there if it is her homeland and he married her there, because of the harm to the child and the Muslim or dhimmi father. Even if both parents are harbi, she has that. It is restricted to the divorced woman, because the married woman does not have the right to go out with him from one town to another absolutely, due to the right of residence of the husband after paying the prompt (mahr). Especially after she went out with him. By 'divorced woman' is meant the (ba'in) after completion of her 'iddah, because the revocably divorced woman has the same ruling as a married woman. And the woman in 'iddah of a ba'in divorce does not have the right to go out before completion of 'iddah absolutely. It is restricted to the mother, because if the mother dies and custody goes to the grandmother, she does not have the right to move to her city. It is valid when the woman is trustworthy, capable of guarding, with no difference between virgin and non-virgin because he is made a supervisor for the Muslims. Al-Ayni and others mentioned it, end. Some virtuous people said, and it is mentioned in the commentary of al-Zayla'i. Al-Shalabi said: It is appropriate to act upon it, especially in this time. And Allah is the Giver of success.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the mother's right to travel with her child after divorce. The author discusses two key conditions: the mother may not travel with the child to a city where she was not married, and there is disagreement if she wants to go to a city other than her own where the marriage took place. According to al-Hidayah, she may not, while al-Jami' al-Saghir says she may because the marriage contract obligates its effects in that place. The preferred view is that marriage in a foreign land does not entail staying there customarily. The author then critiques the use of the term 'safar' (travel) and clarifies that the correct expression is 'khuruj' (moving) with the condition that the distance is significant. He adds exceptions: moving from a village to a city is allowed for the child's benefit, but moving to a hostile territory (dar al-harb) is not allowed if the father is Muslim or dhimmi, unless both parents are harbi. The ruling is restricted to the divorced mother (ba'in after 'iddah), not a married woman or one in 'iddah. The grandmother also does not have the right to move the child. The passage ends with a note that the relied-upon view is what is mentioned in al-Zayla'i's commentary.

Key Terms
حضانة (haḍānah): Custody of the child, the right to raise and care for the child until a certain age.
سفر (safar): Travel; here the author distinguishes between legal travel (safar shar'i, three days journey) and linguistic travel (any moving from one place to another). The correct term for custody removal is 'khuruj' (moving) not 'safar'.
مطلقة (muṭallaqah): A divorced woman. The text specifies that only the divorced woman (ba'in after 'iddah) is considered, not a married woman or one in 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A divorced mother may not move her child to a distant city unless it benefits the child (e.g., village to city) or the distance is close; the preferred Hanafi view restricts such moves.
