Mother's and Father's Rights to Move the Child: Conditions and Dispute
Clarifies conditions for moving child in custody, with intra-madhhab dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mother may move the child from the father's town and identify the Hanafi position on the father's right to move the child, including the dispute with al-Ramli.

Core Idea
The mother may move the child to a nearby city or to the place of marriage; the father may move between nearby villages but not cut the mother's access.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq clarifies the custody rules for moving a child. The key is balancing the mother's right to relocate with the father's right of access. We will examine the conditions for both parents and the scholarly disagreement between Ibn Nujaym and al-Ramli over whether the mother may move to a nearby city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute over 'safar'
Note that the author distinguishes linguistic travel (any distance) from legal travel (three days). The mother may move if the distance allows the father to return in a day.
This clarification is important because legal travel would normally restrict concessions.
2
Understand the mother's conditions for moving
The mother may move the child: (1) to the city where the marriage took place, (2) from a village to a nearby city absolutely. She may not move to a distant village or dar al-harb unless both parents are harbi.
Al-Ramli objects to the exception for a nearby city, arguing it contradicts the madhhab.
3
Apply the conditions for the father
The father may move the child between nearby villages as long as the mother can see the child daily. He may not move the child from the city to villages without the mother's consent due to harm from rural habits.
The father also cannot take the child from the mother's town while she has custody.
4
Analyze the disagreement with al-Ramli
Al-Ramli argues that any move by the mother harms the father and contradicts absolute texts. Ibn Nujaym defends the author by saying 'village' means a nearby one, so no harm.
The practical consequence is whether the mother can move to a nearby city without father's permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والذي يظهر عدم الخ ) قال في النهر والظاهر أن المراد بالسفر هنا اللغوي الذي قطع المسافة لا الشرعي ! إذ لا يشترط أن يقصد مسيرة ثلاثة أيام غير أنها لو قربت بحيث يتمكن من مطالعة ولده ويرجع إلى وطنه في يومه جاز لها النقل قوله : (والعبارة الصحيحة إلى قوله وهي واردة على المصنف) قال الرملي : قوله إلا إذا انتقلت الخ مخالف لإطلاق المتون قاطبة وفيه إضرار بالأب فيمنع عنه، ولم نر هذا لغيره بل كلامهم مصرح بخلافه ا هـ . قلت : يجاب بأن مراد المؤلف بالقرية القريبة من المصر بقرينة قوله وليس فيه ضرر بالأب. نعم يبقى الاعتراض عليه في تركه الأمرين اللذين شرطهما المصنف في المتن فالعبارة الصحيحة لها الخروج بالولد من بلده إلى بلدة هو وقد نكحها فيها ومن قرية إلى مصر قريبة مطلقاً وإلا فلا كإخراجه إلى دار الحرب إلا إذا كانا حربيين قوله : (وقيد بالمطلقة قال الرملي والظاهر أن المتوفى عنها زوجها كالمطلقة في ذلك فلا تملك ذلك بلا إذن الأولياء لقيامهم مقام الأب وما فيه إضرار بالولد ظاهر المنع قوله : (وكذا الأب إذا أراد أن يخرجه إلى مثل ذلك أي إذا أراد أن يخرجه من قرية إلى قرية له ذلك ما لم يقطعه من أمه إذا أرادت أن تبصره كل يوم. وقوله وليس له أن يخرجه من المصر إلى القرى الخ». أي لتضرره بتخلقه بأخلاق أهل القرى نظير ما مر فيما لو أردات إخراجه إليها بدون إذن أبيه. وفي النهر : قيد بالأم لأن الأب ليس له إخراج الولد من بلد أمه ما بقي حق الحضانة لها. وقيده في الحاوي القدسي بغير القريب، أما المكان القريب الذي لا يقطعه عنها إذا أرادت أن تنظر ولدها كل يوم فإنه يجوز كما في جانبها وهو حسن ا هـ . وفيه نظر لأن ما في الحاوي لا يدل على أنه فيما إذا كان حق الحضانة لها، وإذا كان حق الحضانة لها ليس له أخذه منها وهو في بلدها فكيف إذا أراد إخراجه فإن في ذلك
His statement: (And what appears is the incorrectness) - He said in al-Nahr: And it appears that what is meant by 'safar' here is linguistic travel that cuts distance, not legal travel, because it is not required that she intend a journey of three days. However, if it is close such that he can supervise his child and return to his homeland in the day, then she may transfer. His statement: (The correct expression up to his statement: and it is an objection on the author) - al-Ramli said: His statement 'except if she moves' contradicts the absolute texts entirely, and it contains harm to the father so it is prevented. I have not seen this from anyone else; rather their statements clearly contradict it. End. I (the author) say: It can be answered that the author (of Kanz) meant by 'village' the one near the city, indicated by his statement 'and there is no harm to the father'. Yes, there remains the objection that he left the two matters that the author (of Kanz) stipulated in the text. So the correct expression is: she may go out with the child from his town to a town where he married her, and from a village to a nearby city absolutely; otherwise no, like taking him to dar al-harb unless both are harbi. His statement: (And it is restricted to the divorced woman) - al-Ramli said: And it appears that the woman whose husband died is like the divorced woman in that, so she does not have that without the permission of the guardians because they stand in place of the father, and whatever involves harm to the child is clearly prevented. His statement: (And likewise the father if he wants to take him to something similar) - i.e., if he wants to take him from a village to a village, he has that as long as he does not cut him off from his mother when she wants to see him every day. And his statement: 'And he does not have the right to take him from the city to the villages' - i.e., because of harm to him acquiring the morals of villagers, similar to what passed regarding if she wanted to take him to them without her father's permission. In al-Nahr: It is restricted by the mother because the father does not have the right to take the child from his mother's town as long as the right of custody remains with her. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi, it is restricted to non-close places. As for a close place that does not cut him off from her when she wants to see her child every day, it is permissible, as in her side, and this is good. End. There is consideration because what is in al-Hawi does not indicate that it is when the right of custody is hers. And if the right of custody is hers, he cannot take him from her while he is in her town, so how can he take him out? For in that...
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage from page 292 continues the discussion on child custody, focusing on the father's rights and obligations. It begins by clarifying that the term 'safar' in this context means linguistic travel, not legal travel (which has a minimum distance of three days). The key point is that if the distance is short enough that the father can visit the child within a day, the mother may move. Then a criticism from al-Ramli is quoted: the author's exception for moving to a city contradicts established texts and harms the father's rights. The commentator (Ibn Nujaym) defends the author's view, saying the village meant is a nearby one. He then gives the correct formulation: the mother may move to a city where the marriage took place, or from a village to a nearby city; otherwise she may not, except for dar al-harb if both parents are harbi. The passage also discusses the case of a widow, who is treated like a divorced woman but must have guardian permission. For the father, he may move the child between nearby villages as long as the mother can see the child daily, but he cannot move the child from the city to villages without the mother's consent, due to harm from rural habits. al-Nahr states that the father cannot take the child from the mother's town while she has custody. The passage ends with a critique of al-Hawi al-Qudsi's restriction to non-close places.

Key Terms
سفر لغوي (safar lughawī): Linguistic travel: any movement from one place to another, regardless of distance, as opposed to legal travel (safar shar'i) which requires a specific distance (three days journey).
متوفى عنها زوجها (mutawaffā ʿanhā zawjuhā): A widow. The text equates her to a divorced woman in custody matters, but she requires guardian permission to move the child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the mother moving the child from a village to a nearby city?
A) Absolutely prohibited
B) Permissible only with father's consent
C) Permissible absolutely, as stated by the author
D) Permissible only if the child is old enough

Key Takeaway
The mother may move the child to a nearby city or the place of marriage; the father may move between villages but not from city to village; both must preserve the other parent's access.

Mother's Right to Move the Child: Conditions and Proofs
Explaining conditions for mother moving child out of father's town

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a divorced mother may move her child and identify the proofs from Hanafi sources that define these restrictions.

Core Idea
The mother may move the child to a nearby village that does not sever the father's daily visitation; otherwise, it is prohibited.

Divorce does not grant the mother unrestricted freedom to relocate with her child. The Hanafi school balances her custodial rights with the father's right to maintain close contact. This lesson examines the key conditions—proximity, consent, and preservation of visitation—and the textual proofs that shape this balanced ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Recognize that the core question is whether a divorced mother may move her child to a different town or village, and what restrictions apply.
The ruling applies only while the mother retains custody (hadanah).
2
State the general rule
The mother may move the child to a nearby village or city where she was married, provided daily visitation by the father is not severed.
Moving to a distant place that prevents regular contact is prohibited.
3
Examine the proofs
Analyze the narrations from Abu Yusuf in al-Muntaqa and the rulings in al-Hawi al-Qudsi that form the dalil for these conditions.
These are transmitted legal opinions (qawl al-sahabi) with authoritative weight.
4
Apply the conditions to scenarios
Distinguish permissible moves (nearby village within same district) from impermissible ones (distant city or village without father's consent).
The father's right to see the child daily is the key measure.
5
Resolve apparent contradictions
Understand how later commentators like al-Ramli reconcile differing texts by specifying that 'village' means a close-by village that does not harm the father's visitation.
This is a common method in Hanafi fiqh to harmonize conflicting statements.

من قرية إلى قرية لها ذلك ما لم تقطعه من أبيه إذا أراد أن يبصر ولده كل يوم، وكذا الأب إذا أراد أن يخرجه إلى مثل ذلك، وليس له أن يخرجه من المصر إلى القرى بغير رضا أمه إذا كان صغيراً ا.هـ. وفي المجمع : ولا يخرج الأب بولده قبل الاستغناء ا هـ . وعلله في الشرح بأنه لما فيه من الإضرار بالأم بإبطال حقها في الحضانة وهو يدل على أن وطنها على المصنف) قال الرملي : قوله إلا إذا انتقلت الخ مخالف لإطلاق المتون الأمرين اللذين شرطهما المصنف في المتن فالعبارة الصحيحة لها الخروج بالولد من بلده إلى بلدة هي وقد نكحها فيها ومن قرية إلى مصر قريبة مطلقاً وإلا فلا كإخراجه إلى دار الحرب إلا إذا كانا حربيين قوله : (وقيد بالمطلقة قال الرملي والظاهر أن المتوفى عنها زوجها كالمطلقة في ذلك فلا تملك ذلك بلا إذن الأولياء لقيامهم مقام الأب وما فيه إضرار بالولد ظاهر المنع قوله : (وكذا الأب إذا أراد أن يخرجه إلى مثل ذلك أي إذا أراد أن يخرجه من قرية إلى قرية له ذلك ما لم يقطعه من أمه إذا أرادت أن تبصره كل يوم. وقوله وليس له أن يخرجه من المصر إلى القرى الخ». أي لتضرره بتخلقه بأخلاق أهل القرى نظير ما مر فيما لو أردات فيما قلنا وهي حادثة الفتوى في زماننا والله أعلم.
The [commentary] from al-Baqqali? about the child because if there is no marriage contract between them, similarly the umm walad when freed does not remove the child from the city where the boy is, because there is no contract between the father and the umm walad; as in Fath al-Qadir. Other than the grandmother is like the grandmother a fortiori. And he made absolute regarding the hometown thus it includes the village, so she may move him from a city to a village where the marriage contract took place and it is her village, as in Sharh al-Tahawi, and this is the text in al-Kafi of al-Hakim al-Shahid; so what is in the Sharh that she does not have that is weak. And he restricted it to the woman because the father does not have the right to remove the child from the mother's town as long as she has a right to custody. He said in al-Zahiriyyah: In al-Muntaqa Ibn Sama'a from Abu Yusuf: A man married a woman in Basra and she bore him a child, then this man took his young child to Kufa and divorced her, and she disputed with him regarding her child and wanted him returned to her. He said: If the husband took him out with her command, he is not obliged to return him, and it is said to her: go to him and take him. He said: If he took him out without her command, he must bring him to her. Ibn Sama'a from Abu Yusuf regarding a man who went out with the woman and her child from Basra to Kufa, then returned the woman to Basra, then divorced her: he must return her child; thus he is held to it for her. Al-Hawi al-Qudsi: When he marries her in a village of a rural district that has villages close to one another, and she wants to go out with her child from one village to another, she has that right as long as she does not sever him from his father when he wants to see his child every day; similarly the father, if he wants to take him out to something similar, and he does not have the right to take him out from the city to the villages without the mother's consent if he is young. In al-Majma': The father should not take out the child before independence. And he explained in the Sharh that because it harms the mother by nullifying her right to custody, and it indicates that her hometown... upon the author. Al-Ramli said: His statement 'except if she moves' etc. contradicts the absolute statements of all the texts, and it harms the father so it is prohibited, and we have not seen this from anyone else; rather their statements explicitly oppose it. I (the commentator) say: The response is that the author's intention by 'village' is the village close to the city, indicated by his statement 'and there is no harm to the father'. Yes, the objection remains regarding his omission of the two conditions that the author stipulated in the text, so the correct expression is: She may go out with the child from his town to the town in which he married her, and from a village to a nearby city absolutely; otherwise not, like taking him out to the abode of war unless both are belligerents. His statement (Restricted to the divorced woman): Al-Ramli said: It appears that the widow is like the divorced woman in this regard, so she does not have that right without the permission of the guardians because they take the father's place, and whatever harms the child is clearly prohibited. His statement (And similarly the father when he wants to take him out to something similar): i.e., if he wants to take him out from a village to a village, he has that right as long as he does not sever him from his mother when she wants to see him every day. And his statement (And he does not have the right to take him out from the city to the villages etc.): i.e., because of the harm to him by adopting the manners of the villagers, similar to what passed regarding if she wanted to take him out without the father's permission. In al-Nahr: He restricted it to the mother because the father does not have the right to remove the child from the mother's town as long as the right of custody remains for her. And al-Hawi al-Qudsi restricted it to what is not near; as for the near place that does not sever him from her when she wants to see her child every day, it is permissible as in her case, and that is good. There is a problem because what is in al-Hawi does not indicate that when she has the right of custody, and when she has the right of custody he cannot take him from her while he is in her town, so how if he wants to take him out? For that, (her custody?) when it falls, he may travel with him. In al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah: He was asked: If the divorced man takes his child from his custodial mother because she got married, may he travel with him? He answered: He may travel with him until his mother's right returns. And it is explicit in what we said, and it is a fatwa issue in our time, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding the mother's right to move the child after divorce. The author discusses various scenarios: the mother may move the child from one city to a village where the marriage contract was made, but this is restricted to her own village. The father cannot remove the child from the mother's town as long as she has custody rights. Narrations from Abu Yusuf clarify that if the father moved the child with the mother's consent, he need not return him; otherwise, he must. The key condition is that the move should not sever the child from the father's ability to see him daily. Al-Hawi al-Qudsi allows transfer between nearby villages but not from city to village without mother's consent. The commentary resolves a seeming contradiction in the author's wording by specifying that 'village' means a nearby village that does not harm the father. The fatwa from al-Sirajiyyah confirms that if the mother remarries and loses custody, the father may travel with the child.

Key Terms
حضانة (hadanah): Custody of the child, right to raise and care for the young child.
مصر (misr): A city or large town, as opposed to a small village.
قرية (qaryah): A village, often within a rural district (rustaq).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) She may move to a foreign country if she remarries.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits the mother to move the child only to a nearby location that preserves the father's right to daily visitation, based on the principle of preventing harm to both parents.

Father's Travel with Child After Mother's Marriage
Fatwa on father's travel with child after mother's marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a divorced father traveling with his child after the mother's remarriage and identify the condition that restores the mother's custody right.

Core Idea
Father may travel with child until mother's marriage ends; her custody right then returns.

This lesson examines a specific fatwa from al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah that addresses a common post-divorce scenario: a divorced father takes his child from the mother after she remarries, and wishes to travel. The ruling clarifies the father's temporary permission and the mother's future right. Understanding this nuance prevents assuming the father has unlimited authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Recognize the scenario: a divorced father takes his child from the mother (custodian) because she remarried. The question is whether he may travel with the child.
This is a real-world fatwa, not a hypothetical; it reflects a common problem.
2
Extract the ruling and its condition
The answer states: 'He may travel with him until the mother's right returns.' Identify that the permission to travel is conditional – it lasts only as long as the mother's custody right is suspended due to her marriage.
The mother's right of custody (ḥaḍānah) returns when her new marriage ends, either by divorce or death of the husband.
3
Understand the temporal scope
The father's permission is not absolute. It ends when the mother becomes eligible for custody again. The fatwa implies that the father must return the child if the mother reclaims custody after her marriage ends.
This aligns with the Hanafi rule that a custodial mother loses custody upon remarriage, but regains it when the marriage ends.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى السراجية : سئل إذا أخذ المطلق ولده من حاضنته لزواجها هل له أن يسافر به؟ فأجاب بأنه له أن يسافر به إلى أن يعود حق أمه ا هـ . وهو صريح فيما قلنا وهي حادثة الفتوى في زماننا والله أعلم.
And in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah: He was asked: If a divorced man takes his child from his custodian (the mother) due to her marriage, may he travel with him? He answered: He may travel with him until the mother's right returns. That is clear in what we said, and it is a modern fatwa in our time, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a fatwa from al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah concerning a divorced father who takes his child from the mother (the custodian) after she remarries. The question is whether the father may travel with the child. The answer is that he may travel with the child until the mother's right of custody returns (which is when the marriage ends). The commentator notes that this fatwa is clear support for the earlier ruling about custody falling away when the custodian marries, and it is a contemporary issue.

Key Terms
حاضنة (hāḍinah): The female custodian of the child, usually the mother after divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A father may travel with his child after the mother's remarriage, but only until the mother's marriage ends and her custody right revives.

Nafaqah: Definition, Causes, and Obligation for the Wife
Linguistic and legal definition of nafaqah, its three causes, and the ruling with proofs for the wife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of nafaqah, identify its three causes, and apply the proofs to determine when it becomes obligatory for the wife.

Core Idea
Nafaqah is wajib upon the husband because the wife is confined for his enjoyment — it is compensation, not charity.

This lesson unpacks the foundational ruling of nafaqah (maintenance) in the Hanafi school. We begin with its linguistic and legal definition, then explore the three causes that make it obligatory: marriage, kinship, and ownership. Special attention is given to the first cause, marriage, where the text provides both scriptural proofs and a rational principle based on confinement. Understanding this principle is key to distinguishing the Hanafi position from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Define nafaqah linguistically and legally
Identify the lexical meaning from the Quranic usages and the Shariah definition as food, clothing, and shelter.
The text clarifies it is not from nufuq (death) or nifaq (hypocrisy).
2
Identify the three causes of nafaqah
List marriage, kinship, and ownership as the triggers for obligation.
The author begins with marriage because it follows the preceding chapters on nikah, talaq, and iddah.
3
Examine the proof for the wife's nafaqah
Analyze the Quranic verses and hadith cited, and understand the principle of compensation for confinement (al-ihtibas).
This principle is used analogically for other confined persons such as judges and charity collectors.
4
Distinguish the Hanafi cause from the Shafi'i cause
Note the practical difference: Hanafi bases on confinement, Shafi'i on the marital tie alone.
This leads to differences in invalid marriage and iddah cases.

باب النفقة في اللغة الإنسان على عياله ونحو ذلك قال تعالى وما منعهم أن تقبل منهم نفقاتهم ﴾ [التوبة : ٥٤] ويقال أنفق الرجل من النفقة قال تعالى لينفق ذو سعة من سخته کره ای [الطلاق: ۷] وأنفق القوم إذا أنفقت سوقهم، وأنفق الرجل إذا ذهب ماله ويقال منه قوله تعالى إذاً لأمسكتم خشية الإنفاق [الإسراء: ۱۰۰] أي خشية الفقر. ويقال نفقت السلعة نفاقاً نقيض كسدت ونفقة الدابة تفوقاً إذا ماتت ؛ كذا في ضياء الحلوم. وبه علم أن النفقة المرادة هنا ليست مشتقة من النفوق بمعنى الهلاك، ولا من النفق ولا من النفاق، بل هي اسم للشيء الذي ينفقه الرجل على عياله. وأما في الشريعة فذكر في الخلاصة قال هشام : سألت محمداً . النفقة قال : قالوا: ونفقة الغير النفقة هي الطعام والكسوة والسكنى ا هـ . تجب على الغير بأسباب ثلاثة : بالزوجية والقرابة .والملك فبدأ بالأول لمناسبة ما تقدم من
نكاح والطلاق والعدة.
تجب النفقة للزوجة على زوجها والكسوة بقدر حالهما. والأصل في ذلك قوله تعالى لينفق ذو سعة من سعته [الطلاق: ۷] وقوله تعالى وعلى المولود له رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف [البقرة: ٢٣٣] وقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام في حجة الوداع ولهن عليكم رزقهن وكسوتهن بالمعروف» وعليه إجماع الأمة، لأن النفقة جزاء الاحتباس فكل من كان محبوساً بحق مقصود لغيره كانت نفقته عليه. أصله القاضي والعامل في الصدقات والمفتي والوالي والمضارب إذا سافر بمال المضاربة والمقاتلة إذا أقاموا لدفع عدوّ المسلمين. واعترض بأن الرهن محبوس لحق المرتهن وهو الاستيفاء ولذا كان أحق به من سائر الغرماء مع أن نفقته على الراهن. وأجيب بأنه محبوس بحق الراهن أيضاً وهو وفاء دينه عنه عند الهلاك مع كونه ملكاً له. أطلق في الزوجة فشمل المسلمة والكافرة الغنية والفقيرة وأطلق في الزوج فشمل الغني والفقير والصغير والكبير بشرط أن يكون للصغير مال وإلا فلا شيء على أبيه لها كما قدمناه في مهرها. ولم يذكر المصنف طريق إيصال النفقة إليها وهو نوعان تمكين وتمليك. فالتمكين متعين فيما إذا كان له طعام وكثير وهو صاحب مائدة فتمكن المرأة من تناول مقدار كفايتها فليس لها أن تطالبه بفرض النفقة وإن لم يكن بهذه الصفة، فإن رضيت أن تأكل معه فيها ونعمت، وإن خاصمته في فرض النفقة يفرض لها بالمعروف وهو التمليك ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وظاهر ما في الذخيرة أن المراد بصاحب الطعام الكثير أن ينفق على من لا تجب عليه نفقته فحينئذ هي متعنتة في طلب الفرض لأنه إذا كان ينفق على من لا تجب عليه نفقته فلا يمتنع من الإنفاق على من عليه نفقته إلا إذا ظهر للقاضي أنه يضربها ولا ينفق عليها فحينئذ يفرض لها النفقة ا هـ. وظاهر ما في غاية البيان أن النفقة المفروضة تصير ملكاً للمرأة إذا دفعها إليها فلها التصرف فيها من بيع وهبة وصدقة وادخار ويدل على ذلك ما في الخلاصة : لو سرقت الكسوة أو هلكت النفقة لا يفرض لها أخرى بخلاف المحارم، ولو فرض لها دراهم وبقي
وقال الشافعي : السبب الزوجية وهي كونها زوجة له، ويبتنى على هذا الأصل أنه لا نفقة على مسلم في نكاح فاسد لانعدام سبب الوجوب وهو حق الحبس الثابت للزوج عليها بسبب النكاح لأن حق الحبس لا يثبت في النكاح الفساد، وكذا النكاح الفاسد ليس بنكاح حقيقة، وكذا في عدة منه وإن ثبت حق الحبس لأنه لم يثبت بسبب النكاح لانعدام حقيقته، وإنما يثبت لتحصين الماء ولأن حال العدة لا يكون أقوى من حال النكاح . ا هـ ملخصاً وسيأتي من المؤلف الكلام على النكاح الفاسد في آخر هذه المقولة قوله : أطلق) في الزوجة الخ) قال الرملي سيأتي أن الصغيرة التي لا توطأ لا نفقة لها فاستغنى عن استثنائها به تأمل قوله : (بشرط أن يكون للصغير مال الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية قال قاضيخان : وإن كانت كبيرة وليس للصغير مال لا تجب على الأب نفقتها ويستدين الأب عليه ثم يرجع على الابن إذا أيسر أقول : هذا إذا كان في تزويج الصغير مصلحة ولا مصلحة في تزويج قاصر ومرضع بالغة حد الشهوة وطاقة الوطء بمهر كثير ولزوم نفقة يقررها القاضي تستغرق ماله إن كان أو يصير ذا دين كثير ونص المذهب أنه إذا عرف الأب بسوء الاختيار مجانة أو فسقاً فالعقد باطل اتفاقاً ؛ صرح به في البحر وغيره وقدمه المصنف في باب الولي ا هـ .
قوله: (وظاهر ما في الذخيرة (الخ أقول : عبارة الذخيرة البرهانية : وإن كان يعني الزوج حاضراً أو كان صاحب مائدة فالقاضي لا يفرض لها النفقة وإن طلبت لأنها متعنتة في طلب النفقة
Chapter of Nafaqah. In language, (nafaqah) is what a man spends on his dependents, etc. Allah says: 'And what prevented them that their expenditures should be accepted from them?' (9:54). It is said: a man spent from nafaqah. Allah says: 'Let the wealthy spend from his wealth' (65:7). And 'the people spent' when their market is active, and 'the man spent' when his money goes. And from it is His saying: 'then you would withhold, fearing spending' (17:100), i.e., fearing poverty. And it is said: the commodity sold briskly, opposite of becoming stagnant, and the animal died (nafaqa), as in Diya' al-Hulum. From this it is known that the intended nafaqah here is not derived from death, nor from tunnel, nor from hypocrisy, but it is a name for the thing a man spends on his dependents. In Shariah, it is mentioned in al-Khulasah: Hisham said: I asked Muhammad about nafaqah. He said: They said: Nafaqah of another is food, clothing, and dwelling. It is obligatory upon another due to three causes: by marriage, by kinship, and by ownership. He began with the first due to its relevance to what preceded of marriage, divorce, and 'iddah. The nafaqah is obligatory for the wife upon her husband, and clothing according to their condition. The basis for this is Allah's saying: 'Let the wealthy spend from his wealth' (65:7), and His saying: 'And upon the father is their provision and clothing according to what is acceptable' (2:233), and his saying, peace be upon him, in the Farewell Pilgrimage: 'And upon you is their provision and clothing according to what is acceptable.' And upon this is the consensus of the Ummah, because nafaqah is the recompense for confinement. So whoever is confined for a right intended for another, his nafaqah is upon that other. Its origin is the judge, the collector of charity, the mufti, the governor, the mudarib when he travels with the capital of mudarabah, and the fighters when they stay to repel the enemy of Muslims. An objection was raised: The pawn is confined for the right of the mortgagee, which is recovery, so he has more right to it than other creditors, but his nafaqah is upon the pawnor. The answer: He is also confined for the right of the pawnor, which is repayment of his debt upon destruction, while it is his property. It is general regarding the wife, including Muslim and non-Muslim, rich and poor. And it is general regarding the husband, including rich and poor, young and old, on condition that the young one has wealth; otherwise, nothing is upon his father for her, as we presented regarding her dowry. The author did not mention the method of delivering nafaqah to her, which is two types: tamkin (enabling) and tamlik (giving ownership). Tamkin is specific when he has plenty of food and is a person of a table, so he enables the woman to take her sufficiency; then she may not demand a fixed nafaqah. If he is not of this description, then if she agrees to eat with him, well and good; if she disputes with him about fixing nafaqah, it is fixed for her according to what is acceptable, which is tamlik. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. The apparent meaning in al-Dhakhirah is that the meaning of 'person of plenty of food' is that he spends on those whose nafaqah is not obligatory upon him; then she is being difficult in demanding the fixed amount because if he spends on those not obligatory, he will not refrain from spending on those obligatory unless it appears to the judge that he beats her and does not spend on her; then he fixes nafaqah for her. The apparent meaning in Ghayat al-Bayan is that the fixed nafaqah becomes the property of the woman when he pays it to her; she may dispose of it by sale, gift, charity, or saving. Evidence for this is in al-Khulasah: If the clothing is stolen or the nafaqah is destroyed, another is not fixed for her, unlike the case of relatives. And if dirhams are fixed for her and remain... And al-Shafi'i said: The cause is the marital tie, i.e., her being his wife. And upon this principle, there is no nafaqah for a Muslim in an invalid marriage due to the absence of the cause of obligation, which is the right of confinement established for the husband over her due to marriage, because the right of confinement is not established in an invalid marriage, and the invalid marriage is not a true marriage. Likewise in the 'iddah from it, even if the right of confinement is established, because it is not established due to marriage due to the absence of its reality; rather, it is established to preserve the water, and because the state of 'iddah is not stronger than the state of marriage. This is a summary, and the author will discuss the invalid marriage later. His statement: 'It is general regarding the wife, etc.' Al-Ramli said: It will come that the young girl who is not intercourseable has no nafaqah, so he is exempted from mentioning it. Consider his statement: 'On condition that the young one has wealth, etc.' He said in al-Shurunbulaliyyah: Qadi Khan said: If she is an adult and the young one has no wealth, the nafaqah is not obligatory upon the father; the father borrows on his behalf and then recovers from the son when he becomes wealthy. I say: This is if there is benefit in marrying the young one, and there is no benefit in marrying a minor or a breastfeeding adult who has reached the threshold of desire and the capacity for intercourse with a large dowry and the obligation of nafaqah fixed by the judge that consumes his wealth if he has it, or makes him heavily indebted. The clear ruling of the madhhab is that if the father is known for bad choice, frivolity, or immorality, the contract is void by agreement; this is stated in al-Bahr and elsewhere, and the author presented it in the chapter on the guardian. His statement: 'And the apparent meaning in al-Dhakhirah, etc.' I say: The wording of al-Dhakhirah al-Burhaniyyah: If the husband is present or is a person of a table, the judge does not fix nafaqah for her even if she requests it, because she is being difficult in demanding nafaqah.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of nafaqah (maintenance) in Hanafi fiqh. It begins with the linguistic definition of nafaqah, clarifying that it is not derived from words meaning death or hypocrisy but from spending on dependents. In Shariah, nafaqah is defined as food, clothing, and shelter. The causes of nafaqah are three: marriage, kinship, and ownership. The commentator focuses on the first cause, marriage. The ruling: nafaqah is wajib for the wife upon her husband, including food and clothing according to their respective conditions. The primary proofs are Quranic verses (65:7, 2:233) and the hadith of the Prophet in the Farewell Pilgrimage, along with consensus of the Ummah. The rationale is that nafaqah is compensation for confinement: the wife is confined for the husband's right of istimta' (enjoyment), so her maintenance is on him. This principle applies analogically to other cases like judges, charity collectors, etc. An objection regarding the pawn is answered. The ruling covers all wives, regardless of religion or wealth, but the husband must have means; if the husband is a minor with no wealth, neither he nor his father is liable. The method of delivery is either tamkin (enabling the wife to take from the house) or tamlik (giving her ownership of a fixed amount). The difference between these is explained, and the conditions for each are discussed with reference to Ghayat al-Bayan and al-Dhakhirah. The passage also addresses the Shafi'i position that the cause is merely marriage, not the right of confinement, leading to differences in rulings on nafaqah in invalid marriages and for minors. The commentator notes that al-Shafi'i's position results in no nafaqah in an invalid marriage, while the Hanafi position distinguishes based on confinement. The passage concludes with a discussion on the minor's nafaqah when the father arranges marriage, referencing Qadi Khan and the conditions for the marriage to be valid.

Key Terms
النفقة (al-nafaqah): Linguistically, what a man spends on his family. In Shariah, food, clothing, and shelter for those entitled.
الاحتباس (al-ihtibās): Confinement of a person for another's right, e.g., wife confined for husband's enjoyment.
التمكين والتمليك (al-tamkīn wa al-tamlīk): Two methods of delivering nafaqah: tamkin (enabling the wife to take from the home) and tamlik (giving ownership of a fixed amount).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the cause of nafaqah for the wife?
A) The marital tie alone
B) The right of confinement (haqq al-habs)
C) The wife's poverty
D) The husband's wealth

Key Takeaway
Nafaqah is wajib upon the husband as compensation for the wife's confinement for his right, supported by Quran, hadith, consensus, and analogy.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The Wife's Ownership of Nafaqah: Tamlik vs. Ibahah
Explains the two methods of delivering nafaqah and establishes tamlik as the rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamkin and tamlik as methods of delivering nafaqah and state the Hanafi ruling that the wife owns the fixed maintenance absolutely.

Core Idea
The wife's nafaqah, once fixed or paid, becomes her property (tamlik) — she may dispose of it freely.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq elucidates the Hanafi ruling that maintenance (nafaqah) is not merely permission to use the husband's wealth but ownership (tamlik). The author explains the two methods of delivery—tamkin (enabling) and tamlik (ownership)—and clarifies that when the judge fixes maintenance or the husband pays it, the wife becomes the full owner. This has practical consequences: she can sell, gift, or save it, and even if it is lost, no replacement is due. The passage also addresses a key intra-madhhab nuance: although the wife owns the money, the husband normally manages the spending unless he is delaying, because he is the qawwam. This distinction between ownership and management is crucial for understanding the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two methods of delivery
The passage states two methods: tamkin (enabling the wife to eat from the husband's food) and tamlik (giving her ownership of a specific amount). Recognize the scenarios for each.
Tamkin applies when the husband is 'sahib ma'idah' (has abundant food and spends on others).
2
Understand when tamlik applies
When the wife is not enabled (e.g., husband not 'sahib ma'idah'), or if she disputes, the judge fixes maintenance as tamlik.
The fixed or paid amount becomes the wife's property.
3
Recognize the evidence for tamlik
Evidence from al-Khulasa: if the maintenance clothing is stolen, no replacement is fixed (unlike for relatives). Also, compromise after fixing is treated as a sale, proving ownership.
These show the wife owns it; otherwise, loss would be borne by husband.
4
Note the management nuance
Though she owns it, the husband normally undertakes spending (qawwam). Only if he delays does the judge order payment to her. This does not negate ownership.
She may still dispose of surplus, e.g., save or give charity.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i position
Shafi'i holds the basis is marriage itself; Hanafi holds it is the right of detention (haqq al-habs). This affects void marriage: Hanafi says no nafaqah in fasid nikah, Shafi'i says yes.
The dispute shows the practical importance of the underlying sabab.

أطلق في الزوجة فشمل المسلمة والكافرة الغنية والفقيرة وأطلق في الزوج فشمل الغني والفقير والصغير والكبير بشرط أن يكون للصغير مال وإلا فلا شيء على أبيه لها كما قدمناه في مهرها. ولم يذكر المصنف طريق إيصال النفقة إليها وهو نوعان تمكين وتمليك. فالتمكين متعين فيما إذا كان له طعام وكثير وهو صاحب مائدة فتمكن المرأة من تناول مقدار كفايتها فليس لها أن تطالبه بفرض النفقة وإن لم يكن بهذه الصفة، فإن رضيت أن تأكل معه فيها ونعمت، وإن خاصمته في فرض النفقة يفرض لها بالمعروف وهو التمليك ؛ كذا في غاية البيان. وظاهر ما في الذخيرة أن المراد بصاحب الطعام الكثير أن ينفق على من لا تجب عليه نفقته فحينئذ هي متعنتة في طلب الفرض لأنه إذا كان ينفق على من لا تجب عليه نفقته فلا يمتنع من الإنفاق على من عليه نفقته إلا إذا ظهر للقاضي أنه يضربها ولا ينفق عليها فحينئذ يفرض لها النفقة ا هـ. وظاهر ما في غاية البيان أن النفقة المفروضة تصير ملكاً للمرأة إذا دفعها إليها فلها التصرف فيها من بيع وهبة وصدقة وادخار ويدل على ذلك ما في الخلاصة : لو سرقت الكسوة أو هلكت النفقة لا يفرض لها أخرى بخلاف المحارم، ولو فرض لها دراهم وبقي وقال الشافعي : السبب الزوجية وهي كونها زوجة له، ويبتنى على هذا الأصل أنه لا نفقة على مسلم في نكاح فاسد لانعدام سبب الوجوب وهو حق الحبس الثابت للزوج عليها بسبب النكاح لأن حق الحبس لا يثبت في النكاح الفساد، وكذا النكاح الفاسد ليس بنكاح حقيقة، وكذا في عدة منه وإن ثبت حق الحبس لأنه لم يثبت بسبب النكاح لانعدام حقيقته، وإنما يثبت لتحصين الماء ولأن حال العدة لا يكون أقوى من حال النكاح . ا هـ ملخصاً وسيأتي من المؤلف الكلام على النكاح الفاسد في آخر هذه المقولة قوله : أطلق) في الزوجة الخ) قال الرملي سيأتي أن الصغيرة التي لا توطأ لا نفقة لها فاستغنى عن استثنائها به تأمل قوله : (بشرط أن يكون للصغير مال الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية قال قاضيخان : وإن كانت كبيرة وليس للصغير مال لا تجب على الأب نفقتها ويستدين الأب عليه ثم يرجع على الابن إذا أيسر أقول : هذا إذا كان في تزويج الصغير مصلحة ولا مصلحة في تزويج قاصر ومرضع بالغة حد الشهوة وطاقة الوطء بمهر كثير ولزوم نفقة يقررها القاضي تستغرق ماله إن كان أو يصير ذا دين كثير ونص المذهب أنه إذا عرف الأب بسوء الاختيار مجانة أو فسقاً فالعقد باطل اتفاقاً ؛ صرح به في البحر وغيره وقدمه المصنف في باب الولي ا هـ . قوله: (وظاهر ما في الذخيرة (الخ أقول : عبارة الذخيرة البرهانية : وإن كان يعني الزوج حاضراً أو كان صاحب مائدة فالقاضي لا يفرض لها النفقة وإن طلبت لأنها متعنتة في طلب النفقة منها شيء يفرض بخلاف المحارم ا بين وبين هي. وفي الذخيرة لو فرض لها القاضي عشرة دراهم نفقة شهر فمضى الشهر وقد بقي من العشرة شيء يفرض لها القاضي عشرة أخرى، وفرق النفقة الكسوة كما سنبينه في الكسوة. ويدل عليه أيضاً ما فيها : أنهما لو اصطلحا بعد فرض النفقة على شيء لا يصلح تقديراً للنفقة كان معاوضة كالعبد فلولا أنها ملكت النفقة المفروضة لما كان معاوضة. وفي القنية : قال لها خذي هذه الدنانير الخمسة لنفقتك ولم يعين الوقت فهو تمليك لا إباحة ا.هـ. فيفيد أنها تملك النفقة بفرض القاضي أو بدفع شيء بالرضا لكن في الخلاصة والذخيرة إذا فرض القاضي النفقة فالزوج هو الذي يلي الإنفاق إلا إذا ظهر عند القاضي مطله فحينئذ يفرض النفقة ويأمره ليعطيها لتنفق على نفسها نظراً لها فإن لم يعط حبسه ولا تسقط عنه النفقة ا هـ . فهي وإن ملكتها بالفرض لم تتصرف فيها بالانفاق . كما الخلاصة وتفرع على هذا ما لو قرر لها كل يوم مثلاً قدراً معيناً من النفقة فأمرته بإنفاق البعض وأرادت أن تمسك الباقي فمقتضى التمليك أن لها ذلك كما تقدم التصريح به عن والذخيرة في نفقة الشهر، ولا فرق بين نفقة شهر أو يوم فليس فائدة أنه يلي الإنفاق مع فرض القاضي إلا لكونه قواماً عليها لا لأنه يأخذ ما فضل. وعلى هذا لو أمرأته بشراء طعام فاشترى لها فأكلت وفضل شيء واستغنى عنه في يومها فليس له أكله والتصرف فيه إليها هو مقتضى التمليك. ويدل عليه أيضاً أنها لو أسرفت في نفقة الشهر فأكلتها قبل مضيه واحتاجت لا يفرض لها أخرى كما لو هلكت كما في الذخيرة. فالحاصل أن المفروضة أو المدفوعة إليها ملك لها فلها الإطعام منها والتصدق وفي الخانية : المرأة إذا فرضت لها النفقة من مال نفسها أو من مسألة الناس كان لها أن ترجع بالمفروض على زوجها ا هـ. . وفي البدائع : : وإذا طلبت المرأة من القاضي فرض النفقة قبل النقلة وهي بحيث لا تمتنع من التسليم لو طالبها بالتسليم أو كان امتناعها لحق فرض القاضي لها إعانة لها على الوصول إلى حقها الواجب، وإن كان بعدما حولها إلى منزله فزعمت عدم الإنفاق أو التضييق فلا ينبغي له أن يعجل بالفرض ولكن يأمره بالنفقة والتوسيع إلى أن يظهر ظلمه، فحينئذ يفرض عليه النفقة ويأمره أن يدفعها إليها لتنفق على نفسها ولو طلبت كفيلاً بها خوفاً من غيبته لا يجبره لأن الرجل إذا كان بهذه الصفة ينفق على من ليس عليه نفقته فلا يمتنع من الإنفاق على من عليه نفقته فلا يفرض لها القاضي إلا إذا ظهر للقاضي أنه يضربها ولا ينفق عليها فحينئذ يفرض لها النفقة، وإن لم يكن الزوج صاحب مائدة فالقاضي يفرض لها النفقة كل شهر ا.هـ. وهو كما ترى لا يدل على ما ادعى والذي يدل كلامه عليه أنه إذا ظهر للقاضي تعنتها بأي طريق من الطرق لا يفرض من غير أن يكون إنفاقه على من لا يجب عليه إنفاقه شرطاً وذلك لا يتوهم ذو فهم مع قوله إلا إذا ظهر للقاضي أنه يضربها تأمل ( رملي) قوله : (فهي وإن ملكتها بالفرض لم تتصرف أي ليس لها التصرف فيها
He (the author) spoke generally concerning the wife, so it includes the Muslim and the disbeliever, the rich and the poor. And he spoke generally concerning the husband, so it includes the rich and the poor, the minor and the adult, on condition that the minor has wealth; otherwise nothing is owed by his father to her, as we have already mentioned regarding her dowry. And the author did not mention the method of delivering maintenance to her, and it is of two types: enabling (tamkin) and ownership (tamlik). Enabling is obligatory when he has much food and is the master of a table; then the woman is enabled to take her sufficient amount, so she may not demand a fixed maintenance from him. If he is not of this description, and she agrees to eat with him, then fine; but if she disputes with him regarding fixing maintenance, it is fixed for her in a fair manner, which is ownership (tamlik). This is stated in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the apparent meaning of what is in al-Dhakhira is that what is meant by 'master of much food' is that he spends on those whose maintenance is not obligatory upon him. In that case, she is being vexatious in demanding a fixed maintenance, because if he spends on those for whom maintenance is not obligatory, he will not refrain from spending on one for whom it is obligatory, unless it becomes apparent to the judge that he beats her and does not spend on her; then the judge fixes maintenance for her. And the apparent meaning of what is in Ghayat al-Bayan is that the fixed maintenance becomes the property of the woman when he gives it to her, so she may dispose of it by sale, gift, charity, and savings. Evidence for that is what is in al-Khulasa: if the clothing is stolen or the maintenance is lost, another is not fixed for her, unlike the case of relatives. And if he fixed dirhams for her and something remained... And al-Shafi'i said: the cause is marriage, i.e., her being his wife. Upon this principle it is built that there is no maintenance for a Muslim in a void marriage (nikah fasid) due to the absence of the cause of obligation, which is the right of detention (haqq al-habs) established for the husband over her due to marriage, because the right of detention is not established in a void marriage, and likewise the void marriage is not a marriage in reality. Similarly, in the 'idda from it, even if the right of detention is established, it is not established due to marriage because its reality is absent; rather, it is established to protect the water (i.e., sperm), and because the state of 'idda cannot be stronger than the state of marriage. Summarized. And the author will discuss void marriage later in this statement. He said: (He spoke generally) concerning the wife etc. Al-Ramli said: It will come later that the minor who is not sexually penetrated has no maintenance, so he dispensed with exempting her by it. Consider. He said: (On condition that the minor has wealth etc.) said in al-Shurnubalal: Qadi Khan said: If she is major and the minor has no wealth, maintenance is not obligatory upon the father; but the father may borrow on his (the minor's) behalf, then reclaim from the son when he becomes wealthy. I say: this is when there is benefit in marrying the minor; there is no benefit in marrying a minor and breastfeeding an adult who has reached the limit of desire and ability for intercourse with a high dowry and ongoing maintenance fixed by the judge that consumes his wealth or makes him heavily indebted. The explicit madhhab is that if the father is known for bad choice, or foolishness, or transgression, the contract is void by agreement; as stated in al-Bahr and others, and the author presented it in the chapter on the guardian. He said: (And the apparent meaning of what is in al-Dhakhira etc.) I say: the phrasing of al-Dhakhira al-Burhaniyya: If the husband is present or is the master of a table, the judge does not fix maintenance for her even if she demands it, because she is being vexatious in demanding maintenance. Something remains fixed unlike the relatives. And in al-Dhakhira: if the judge fixed for her ten dirhams as maintenance for a month, then the month passed and something remained from the ten, the judge fixes another ten for her. And the same for clothing, as we will explain in the section on clothing. Evidence for that is also what is in it: if they made a compromise after fixing maintenance on something that does not serve as a valuation of maintenance, it is a barter transaction like a slave; for if she had not owned the fixed maintenance, it would not be a barter. And in al-Quniyya: he said to her 'Take these five dinars for your maintenance' without specifying time; it is ownership (tamlik), not permission (ibahah). So it indicates that she owns the maintenance either by the judge's fixing or by paying something by mutual consent. But in al-Khulasa and al-Dhakhira: if the judge fixes maintenance, the husband is the one who undertakes spending, unless it becomes apparent to the judge that he is delaying, then he fixes maintenance and orders him to give it to her so that she may spend on herself as a consideration for her. If he does not give, he is imprisoned, but the maintenance is not dropped. So although she owns it by the fixing, she does not dispose of it by spending. As in al-Khulasa. And derived from this is: if he fixed for her every day, for example, a specific amount of maintenance, and she ordered him to spend part and wanted to keep the remainder, the consequence of ownership is that she has that right, as has been explicitly stated in al-Dhakhira regarding monthly maintenance. And there is no difference between monthly or daily maintenance; the benefit of his undertaking spending with the judge's fixing is only because he is the guardian over her (qawwam), not because he takes the surplus. Based on this, if she ordered him to buy food, and he bought for her, and she ate, and something remained and she did not need it that day, he may not eat it; disposal of it is hers, which is the consequence of ownership. Evidence for that is also that if she wasted the monthly maintenance and ate it before it passed and needed more, another is not fixed for her, just as if it were lost, as in al-Dhakhira. So the conclusion is that the fixed or paid maintenance is her property, so she may feed from it and give charity. And in al-Khaniyya: if the woman has maintenance fixed from her own wealth or from begging, she may reclaim the fixed amount from her husband. And in al-Bada'i: if the woman asks the judge to fix maintenance before the transfer, and she is not refusing to submit if he demands submission, or her refusal is due to a right, then the judge fixing maintenance for her is assistance for her to obtain her obligatory right. But if after he moved her to his house she claims non-spending or hardship, then he should not hasten to fix but order him to spend and be generous until his injustice becomes apparent; then he fixes maintenance and orders him to pay it to her so she can spend on herself. If she demands a guarantor for fear of his absence, he is not compelled, because if a man is of this description, he spends on those for whom maintenance is not obligatory, so he will not refrain from spending on those for whom it is obligatory. So the judge does not fix maintenance for her unless it appears to the judge that he beats her and does not spend; then he fixes maintenance for her. And if the husband is not the master of a table, the judge fixes maintenance for her every month. And it is as you see, it does not indicate what he claimed; rather what his statement indicates is that if the judge becomes aware of her being vexatious in any way, he does not fix it, without requiring that he spends on those for whom it is not obligatory. No one with understanding would suppose that, given his words 'unless it appears to the judge that he beats her'. Consider. (Ramli) His words: (So although she owns it by the fixing, she does not dispose of it) i.e., she does not have the right to dispose of it
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the text concerning the wife's maintenance (nafaqah) and her ownership of it. The author begins by noting that the original text spoke generally about the wife and husband, covering all categories (Muslim, disbeliever, rich, poor, minor, adult), but with the condition that a minor husband must have wealth for his wife's maintenance to be obligatory on his father. Then it discusses the two methods of delivering maintenance: tamkin (enabling the wife to eat from the husband's food) and tamlik (giving her ownership of the maintenance amount). Tamkin is used when the husband has abundant food and is 'sahib ma'idah' (master of a table); in that case the wife cannot demand a fixed sum. If she demands it, the judge fixes it for her as tamlik. The commentary from al-Dhakhira clarifies that 'sahib ma'idah' means he spends on non-obligatory persons, so she is vexatious in demanding a fix; only if the judge sees beating or non-payment does he fix it. The core ruling is from Ghayat al-Bayan: the fixed maintenance becomes the wife's property (tamlik), and she can sell, gift, or save it. Evidence is adduced from al-Khulasa that if clothing is stolen or maintenance lost, no replacement is due (unlike for relatives), proving ownership. Even if she had dirhams left over, the next month's fix is still set. The passage includes a brief inter-madhhab dispute: Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the cause (sabab) of maintenance is marriage (zawjiyya), whereas the Hanafi position (implicitly) is that it is the right of detention (haqq al-habs). This difference leads to practical outcomes regarding void marriage. The commentary then presents various scenarios: if the husband is present and hospitable, no fixed maintenance; if not, the judge fixes it. The discussion of tamlik vs ibahah (ownership vs permission) is central. Several works (al-Zakhirah, al-Khulasa, al-Quniyya, al-Bada'i, al-Khaniyya) are cited to show that the wife owns the nafaqah—she can barter it, feed others, give charity—but there is a nuance: when the judge fixes maintenance, the husband normally undertakes spending; only if he delays is the wife given the money. This is not a contradiction: ownership does not necessarily entail physical possession, since the husband is the guardian (qawwam), but the wife has full proprietary rights. The passage concludes with a remark from Ramli correcting a misinterpretation: the condition for not fixing is not that the husband spends on non-obligatory persons, but rather that the wife is being vexatious. This clarifies the Hanafi position that the wife owns the fixed maintenance, and the judge's role is to ensure her right is protected.

Key Terms
تمكين (tamkin): Enabling the wife to eat from the husband's food without giving her ownership of a specific amount.
تمليك (tamlik): Giving the wife ownership of a specific amount of maintenance, which she may dispose of freely.
صاحب مائدة (sahib ma'idah): A husband who habitually provides food and spends on others, so there is no need to fix maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, what is the nature of the fixed maintenance (nafaqah) given to the wife?
A) It is a mere permission to consume (ibahah) without ownership.
B) It becomes the wife's property (tamlik) which she can sell or give in charity.
C) It remains the husband's property but the wife has use rights.
D) It is a loan that the wife must return if she doesn't spend it.

Key Takeaway
The fixed or paid nafaqah in Hanafi fiqh is tamlik (ownership), not ibahah (permission), giving the wife full proprietary rights over the maintenance amount.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Defining Nashizah: Actual and Constructive Exit
Defines nashizah and explains that nafaqah drops.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define nashizah in Hanafi law and distinguish between actual and constructive exit.

Core Idea
A nashizah is a wife who leaves her husband's house or prevents his access – both actual and constructive exit drop her nafaqah.

In this lesson, we explore the precise Hanafi definition of nashizah from Al-Bahr al-Raiq. The author distinguishes between actual physical departure and constructive refusal of access, both of which nullify the wife's right to maintenance.

Guided Path
1
Linguistic Definition of Nashizah
Start with the linguistic meaning of nushuz: disobedience, hatred, and aversion between spouses. The term is used for either spouse but here focused on the wife.
The text cites al-Zajjaj: nushuz is mutual hatred.
2
Legal Definition by al-Khassaf
The legal definition: a wife who leaves her husband's house (khuruj haqiqi) or prevents herself from him (man' al-nafs) without valid excuse.
Al-Khassaf was a leading Hanafi jurist.
3
Two Types of Khuruj: Actual and Constructive
Actual exit is physically leaving the house. Constructive exit (khuruj hukmi) is remaining in the house but not allowing the husband entry or access.
Constructive exit is considered as if she left; the husband cannot fulfill his rights.
4
Link to Nafaqah: Taslim as a Condition
Nafaqah is wajib only when the wife submits herself (taslim/takhliya). Nushuz violates this condition, so nafaqah drops.
Submission means removing barriers to enjoyment.

Source Text

كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة بالمعلجل لأنه لو كان كله مؤجلاً فامتنعت فلا نفقة لها لأنه نشوز كما في غاية البيان، وقدمنا أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف من أن لها المنع فعلى هذا لا تسقط نفقتها لأنه بحق. وأشار الدخول في منزلها الذي يسكنان فيه قبل أن تسأله النقلة الخ) في الفتاوى الهندية ولا نفقة في النكاح الفاسد ولا في العدة منه، ولو كان النكاح صحيحاً من حيث الظاهر ففرض القاضي لها النفقة وأخذت ذلك شهراً ثم كذا في الذخيرة. وأجمعوا أن في النكاح بغير شهود تستحق النفقة؛ كذا في الخلاصة ا هـ . قلت : والظاهر أن الصواب لا تستحق إذ لا شك أن النكاح بلا شهود فاسد والنفقة إنما تستحق بالاحتباس ولا احتباس في الفاسد كما قدمناه أول هذا الباب عن البدائع قوله : (وأشار المصنف إلى أن شرط وجوب النفقة تسليم المرأة (الخ أما إذا لم تسلم نفسها إليه وقت وجوب التسليم فلا تجب النفقة . لأنها كالخارجة. وعلله في الذخيرة بأنها ليس منزلاً له أصلاً بخلاف البيت الذي فيه شبهة كبيت السلطان ليس لها أن تمتنع وتصير ناشزة كما في الخانية لعدم اعتبار الشبهة في زماننا كما في التجنيس . وقيد بالخروج لأنها لو كانت مقيمة في منزله ولم تمكنه من الوطء فإنها لا تكون ناشزة لأن الظاهر أن الزوج يقدر على تحصيل المقصود منها بدليل أن البكر لا توطأ إلا كرها وقد علم مما قدمناه أن المراد بمنعها نفسها منه المنع بغير حق فلذا قال في الخلاصة : لو كان الزوج بسمرقند وكانت زوجته بنسف فبعث إليها أجنبياً ليحملها إلى سمرقند ولم تذهب معه لعدم المحرم فإن لها النفقة. وشمل الخروج الحكمي ما إذا طلب أن يسافر بها من بلدها وامتنعت فإنه لا نفقة لها على ظاهر الرواية من أن له السفر بها، وأما على المفتى به فإنها لا تكون ناشزة كما قدمناه وأشار إليه في الذخيرة هنا. وأطلق في عدم وجوب النفقة للناشزة فشمل ما إذا كانت النفقة مفروضة فإن النشوز يسقطها أيضاً إلا إذا استدانت فإن المستدانة لا يسقطها النشوز على أصح الروايتين كالموت لا يسقطها أيضاً كما في الذخيرة وهو مما ينبغي حفظه. ولم يذكر ما إذا تركت النشوز وهو بعودها إلى منزله لظهور أن النفقة تعود لأنه من باب زوال المانع. وفي الخلاصة : الناشزة إذا عادت إلى بيت الزوج بعدما سافر زوجها أجابوا أنها خرجت عن أن تكون ناشزة ا هـ. وشمل تعريف الناشزة المنكرة للنكاح فإذا ادعى عليها النكاح فجحدت ثم أقام البينة فلا نفقة لها. زاد في فتح القدير : وكذا إذا كان الزوج هو المنكر ثم قال : ولقائل أن يقول أن يجب لأنها صارت مكذبة شرعاً، وكذا الزوج وإلا فلا يخفى ما فيه من الإضرار وفتح باب الفساد خصوصاً عند اضطرارها للنفقة مع حبسها اهـ . ولا يخفى أنهم إنما نفوا النفقة ما دامت جاحدة، أما إذا عادت إلى التصديق وطلبت النفقة فإن لها النفقة وأما إذا كان الزوج هو المنكر فإنما نفوا وجوب النفقة عنه في مدة المسألة عن الشهود لا مطلقاً كما سنبينه بعد ذلك عن الظهيرية. وخرج عنه ما إذا أجرت نفسها لإرضاع صبي وزوجها شريف ولم تخرج من منزله وذكر في الفوائد التاجية نقلين فيها : الثاني منهما كما ذكرنا، والأول هو نشوز وإن لم تخرج ولا يخفي ضعفه. وفي الخلاصة : إن قال الزوج هي ناشزة فلا نفقة لها علي فإن شهدوا أنه أوفاها المعجل وهي لم تكن في بيت الزوج سقطت النفقة، ولو شهدوا أنها ليست في طاعة الزوج للجماع لا تقبل لأنه يحتمل أنها تكون في بيته وينبغي وجوب: وجب، ولا قوله : (إلا إذا استدانت الخ قال الرملي الكلام في الوجوب لا في إسقاط ما . شبهة في أن الناشزة لا تجب نفقتها مطلقاً فكلام المختصر على إطلاقه وكلام هذا الشارح فيه نظر
Book of Divorce, Chapter of Maintenance: With the prompt [mahr] because if it were all deferred and she refused, then no maintenance for her because it is nushuz as in Ghayat al-Bayan, and we have previously stated that the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf that she has the right to refuse; based on this, her maintenance does not drop because it is with right. And the author indicated that the condition for the obligation of maintenance is the woman's submission of herself to the husband at the time when submission is obligatory, and by submission we mean takhliya, i.e., she leaves between herself and her husband free access by removing the barrier to intercourse or enjoyment, if the barrier is from her side or from other than the husband. So if he marries an adult free healthy sound woman and transfers her to his house, she has maintenance; likewise if he does not transfer her and she is such that she does not prevent herself and she requests maintenance and he does not demand transfer, then she has maintenance. If he demands transfer and she refuses, then if her refusal is with right, such as refusing to collect her prompt mahr, then she has maintenance. Likewise if he demands transfer after paying the mahr to a usurped house and she refuses, then she has maintenance because it is with right. And if she was living in her house and prevented him from entering, not as nushuz, but said: 'Move me to your house or rent a house for me to live in, for I need this house of mine to take its rent,' then she has maintenance; so in al-Bada'i'. And in al-Dhakhira: Some later scholars from the imams of Balkh said: She does not deserve maintenance if she has not been transferred to the husband's house, and the fatwa is on the answer of the book, i.e., maintenance if he has not demanded transfer from her. Obligation? His saying: (لا ناشزة) [no nashizah] in the genitive case as an adjective to 'the wife', i.e., maintenance is not obligatory for the nashizah, and she linguistically is the one who disobeys her husband and hates him. It is said: 'The woman nashazat against her husband, so she is nashizah.' And from al-Zajjaj: Nushuz occurs between the spouses, and it is the hatred of each one of them for the other; so in al-Mughrib. And in the Sacred Law, as Imam al-Khassaf said: The one who leaves her husband's house and prevents herself from him. And by 'khuruj' (exit) is meant her being in other than his house without his permission, to include the case where she refuses to come to his house initially without payment of her prompt mahr, and the case where she leaves her house after moving into it. And he generalized 'khuruj' to include actual and constructive, which is her not enabling him to enter into her house in which they used to live before she asks for transfer. (End) in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya: And there is no maintenance in an invalid marriage nor in the iddah from it. If the marriage was apparently valid and the judge imposed maintenance and she took it for a month, then the invalidity became apparent (such as witnesses testifying she is his foster sister and the judge separates them), the husband recovers what she took from the woman. But if he spent without a judge's imposition, he does not recover anything; so mentioned by al-Sadr al-Shahid in Sharh Adab al-Qadi, so in al-Dhakhira. And they unanimously agreed that in a marriage without witnesses, maintenance is deserved; so in al-Khulasa. End quote. I say: Apparently the correct view is that it is not deserved, for there is no doubt that marriage without witnesses is invalid, and maintenance is only deserved due to ihtibas (confinement), and there is no ihtibas in an invalid marriage as we have stated at the beginning of this chapter from al-Bada'i'. His saying: (And the author indicated that the condition for the obligation of maintenance is the woman's submission...) As for when she does not submit herself to him at the time when submission is obligatory, then maintenance is not obligatory because she is like one who has left. And al-Dhakhira explains it by saying she has become as if she nashazat to another place, indicating that it is a constructive exit from his house, unlike the case where she prevented him after she asked for transfer, as we have mentioned. And it excludes the case where she left a usurped house or refused to move into it, then she is not nashizah, as we have mentioned, because it is not his house at all. But in the case of a house with a semblance of right, like the sultan's house, she cannot refuse and become nashizah, as in al-Khaniyya, because semblance is not considered in our time, as in al-Tajnis. And he restricted [the definition] to 'khuruj' because if she was residing in his house and did not enable him to have intercourse, then she is not nashizah, because outwardly the husband is able to achieve his objective from her, as evidenced by the fact that a virgin is only had intercourse with reluctantly. And it is known from what we have mentioned that what is meant by her preventing herself from him is prevention without right. Hence al-Khulasa said: If the husband was in Samarqand and his wife in Nasaf, and he sent a stranger to bring her to Samarqand, and she did not go with him due to lack of a mahram, then she has maintenance. And constructive exit includes the case where he asks to travel with her from her country and she refuses, then there is no maintenance for her according to the apparent narration that he has the right to travel with her. But according to the mufta bihi, she is not nashizah, as we have mentioned and as al-Dhakhira indicated here. And he generalized in the non-obligation of maintenance for the nashizah, so it includes even if maintenance was already imposed, then nushuz also drops it, except if she borrowed (i.e., incurred debt for maintenance), then nushuz does not drop it according to the more correct of the two narrations, just as death does not drop it either, as in al-Dhakhira, and it is worth memorizing. And he did not mention the case when she leaves nushuz by returning to his house, as it is obvious that maintenance returns because it is a case of removal of the impediment. And in al-Khulasa: The nashizah, if she returns to the husband's house after he has traveled, they answered that she has ceased to be nashizah. End. And the definition of nashizah includes the one who denies the marriage; so if he claims marriage and she denies, then he brings evidence, then there is no maintenance for her. In Fath al-Qadir it adds: Likewise if the husband is the denier. Then he said: And one could say that it must be [obligatory] because she has become legally falsified, and likewise the husband. Otherwise the harm and opening the door to corruption is not hidden, especially when she is in need of maintenance while being confined. End. It is not hidden that they only denied maintenance as long as she is denier; if she returns to affirmation and requests maintenance, then she has maintenance. And if the husband is the denier, they only denied the obligation of maintenance from him during the period of questioning the witnesses, not absolutely, as we will explain later from al-Zahiriyya. And it excludes the case where she hired herself to nurse a baby while her husband is noble and she did not leave his house; in al-Fawa'id al-Tajiyya there are two narrations: the second as we mentioned, and the first is nushuz even if she did not leave, and its weakness is not hidden. And in al-Khulasa: If the husband says she is nashizah, then there is no maintenance for her from him. If they testify that he paid the prompt mahr while she was not in the husband's house, maintenance drops. And if they testify that she is not in obedience to the husband for intercourse, it is not accepted because it is possible that she is in his house. And it is recommended: obligatory? It becomes obligatory, and no. His saying: (Except if she borrowed...) al-Ramli said: The discussion is about obligation, not about dropping what has been taken. There is a doubt that the nashizah's maintenance is not obligatory at all, so the words of the Mukhtasar are general, and the words of this commentator require scrutiny.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq is a commentary on the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, specifically explaining the ruling on the nashizah (disobedient wife). The passage is a definition_ruling, as it first defines the term nashizah both linguistically and in the Sacred Law, then states the ruling that she does not deserve nafaqah (maintenance). The author (Ibn Nujaym) also discusses conditions for nafaqah, exceptions, and intra-Hanafi differences. 

Key points: 
1. Definition: A nashizah is a wife who leaves her husband's house without his permission or prevents him from accessing her without a valid right. This includes actual exit (leaving the house) and constructive exit (refusing entry to the husband while she is in her own house, if she has not requested a transfer to his house). 
2. Ruling: A nashizah has no right to maintenance. 
3. Condition for maintenance: The wife must submit herself to the husband (taslim/takhliya) by removing any barrier to intercourse or enjoyment. This submission is required at the time when it becomes obligatory, i.e., when the husband demands it or when he has provided a suitable dwelling. 
4. Exceptions where refusal does not constitute nushuz: 
   - She refuses to move to his house until she receives her prompt mahr (mahr mu'ajjal). 
   - She refuses to move to a house that is usurped (ghasb). 
   - She asks to be moved to another house or rent one because she needs the rent from her own house. 
5. Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: According to Abu Yusuf, a wife can refuse to move before receiving mahr, and this does not drop nafaqah. Some later Balkh scholars said she does not deserve nafaqah unless she has been physically transferred to the husband's house, but the fatwa is on the earlier position. 
6. Invalid marriage: No nafaqah in a fasid nikah (invalid marriage) or in its iddah. If the judge imposed nafaqah based on apparent validity and later invalidity is discovered, the husband can recover what he paid. An authorial comment notes that marriage without witnesses is invalid, so nafaqah is not due, contrary to an 'ijma' claim in al-Khulasa. 
7. Effect of nushuz on already imposed maintenance: Nushuz also drops imposed maintenance, except if the wife had borrowed (i.e., incurred debt for maintenance) – that debt is not dropped by nushuz. 
8. When nushuz ends: If the nashizah returns to the husband's house, nafaqah resumes because the impediment is removed. 
9. Denial of marriage: If the wife denies the marriage (and the husband proves it), she is considered nashizah during the denial and has no nafaqah. If the husband is the denier, the ruling differs – some say she still deserves nafaqah because she is legally vindicated, but the passage suggests further detail. 
10. Nursing outside: If a wife hires herself to nurse a baby without leaving her husband's house, some narrations say it is nushuz, but the preferred view is that it is not. 

The passage ends with a critique of a commentator's view on a borrowed maintenance case. The overall discussion emphasizes the interconnectedness of submission, valid excuses, and the legal definition of nushuz.

Key Terms
ناشزة (nāshizah): A wife who leaves her husband's house or prevents him from accessing her without a valid right.
خروج حكمي (khurūj ḥukmī): Constructive exit: the wife remains in the marital home but prevents the husband from entering or enjoying her.
تسليم (taslīm): Submission of the wife to the husband by removing barriers to intercourse and enjoyment (takhliya).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the legal definition of nashizah?
A) A wife who hates her husband.
B) A wife who leaves her husband's house or prevents herself from him.
C) A wife who refuses to obey her husband's commands.
D) A wife who refuses to move to her husband's house without any excuse.

Key Takeaway
A nashizah is defined by actual or constructive exit without valid right; her nafaqah is suspended.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Valid Excuses for Refusing Transfer: When Nafaqah Remains
Explains valid excuses for refusal and the mufta bihi position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the valid grounds upon which a wife may refuse transfer to her husband's house without losing nafaqah, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mahr mu'ajjal.

Core Idea
A wife's refusal to move is excused if she demands her prompt mahr or if the house is usurped; the fatwa allows her to keep nafaqah.

Building on the definition of nashizah, this lesson examines the exceptions: when does a wife's refusal not constitute nushuz? The passage details valid excuses and reports a key disagreement between Abu Yusuf and later Balkh scholars.

Guided Path
1
Basic Rule and Exceptions
Normally, refusal to transfer to the husband's house constitutes nushuz, but there are exceptions.
2
Exception 1: Refusal until Mahr Mu'ajjal is Paid
If the wife refuses to move until she receives her prompt mahr, her refusal is with right and she is not nashizah.
This is based on Abu Yusuf's view, which is the fatwa.
3
Exception 2: Husband's House is Usurped (Ghasb)
If the husband demands transfer to a house that he has taken by force, the wife may refuse without losing nafaqah.
Because it is not truly his house.
4
Exception 3: Wife Needs Rental Income
If the wife lives in her own house and needs its rent, she may ask to be moved to another house or to have one rented, and refusal until then is not nushuz.
She is not preventing access maliciously.
5
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Abu Yusuf vs. Balkh Scholars
Some later Balkh scholars argued that nafaqah only begins after the wife is physically transferred to the husband's home (zifaf). Abu Yusuf held that nafaqah is due once the husband demands transfer and the wife submits, but if she refuses for a valid reason, it remains. The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if a wife refuses to move without paying mahr, Abu Yusuf says she gets nafaqah; Balkh scholars say no.

كتاب الطلاق باب النفقة بالمعلجل لأنه لو كان كله مؤجلاً فامتنعت فلا نفقة لها لأنه نشوز كما في غاية البيان، وقدمنا أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف من أن لها المنع فعلى هذا لا تسقط نفقتها لأنه بحق. وأشار الرواية من أن له السفر بها، وأما على المفتى به فإنها لا تكون ناشزة كما قدمناه وأشار إليه في الذخيرة هنا. وأطلق في عدم وجوب النفقة للناشزة فشمل ما إذا كانت النفقة مفروضة فإن النشوز يسقطها أيضاً إلا إذا مع حبسها اهـ . ولا يخفى أنهم إنما نفوا النفقة ما دامت جاحدة، أما إذا عادت إلى التصديق وطلبت النفقة فإن لها النفقة وأما إذا كان الزوج هو المنكر فإنما نفوا وجوب النفقة عنه في مدة المسألة عن الشهود لا مطلقاً كما سنبينه بعد ذلك عن الظهيرية. وخرج عنه ما إذا أجرت نفسها لإرضاع صبي وزوجها شريف ولم تخرج من منزله وذكر في الفوائد التاجية نقلين فيها : الثاني منهما كما ذكرنا، والأول هو نشوز وإن لم تخرج ولا
